Objection on Proving the Maker in Kalam
A single-sentence objection about the status of proving the Maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of an objection raised about proving the Maker being among the highest demands of kalam and why such objections are important in theological debate.

Core Idea
An objection can challenge the very foundation of kalam by questioning its highest aim.

In the study of kalam, objections are not merely obstacles but opportunities to refine arguments. This short passage presents a concise objection that touches on the status of proving the existence of the Creator. Even without the answer, we can begin to analyze the logic and structure of such objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the brevity and the use of 'qala' (he said) to introduce the objection.
The passage is only one sentence, so read it slowly.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the terms 'إثبات الصانع' (proving the Maker) and 'مطالب الكلام' (demands of kalam). Understand that 'a'la matalib' means 'the highest demands'.
3
Consider the objection's force
Think about what it means for proving the Maker to be among the highest demands of kalam. This might imply that kalam's main goal is to prove God's existence, and any other principles must serve that.
The objection likely challenges a previous point about the derivation of kalam's principles.
4
Reflect on the context
Recognize that this objection is incomplete without an answer, but even as a standalone statement, it raises an important methodological issue.
The answer is not in this window, so we only have the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واعترض بأن إثبات الصانع من أعلى مطالب الكلام
He said: And it was objected that proving the Maker is among the highest demands of kalam.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This is a short objection statement, introduced by 'qala' (he said). It objects that proving the existence of the Maker (al-Sani') is among the highest demands (a'la matalib) of kalam. This likely challenges some aspect of the preceding discussion, perhaps implying that if kalam's highest topic is the proof of God, then its principles cannot be dependent on other sciences. However, the answer is not present in this window, so the argument is incomplete.

Key Terms

مطالب الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Proving the Maker is impossible
B) Proving the Maker is among the highest demands of kalam
C) Proving the Maker requires metaphysics
D) Proving the Maker is not a topic of kalam

Key Takeaway
The objection raises the question of how proving the Maker relates to the highest goals of kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an objection to a core belief like God's existence, do you see it as a threat or an opportunity to deepen your conviction? How does this objection, which places proving God at the highest level of kalam, challenge you to take your intellectual journey more seriously?
?
If proving the Maker is one of the ultimate goals of kalam, what does that reveal about your own responsibility to seek certainty about God? Are you actively pursuing that certainty, or are you content with inherited beliefs?

The Sixth Objection and Its Refutation
Refutation of the sixth objection to the proof for the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the sixth objection to the proof for the Creator by clarifying the meaning of a proof's 'indication' and addressing a further objection.

Core Idea
A proof's validity does not require its constant existence or consideration.

In this passage, the author faces a sophisticated objection: if a proof for the Creator is valid, what exactly does it imply? The objector forces a dilemma, but the author clarifies what we mean by a proof's indication. He then tackles a further objection about the circularity of proving that proofs are valid. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the author's initial response
First, read the objector's dilemma and the author's quick reply. Focus on the core issue: what does a proof 'give' or 'indicate'? Notice that the author rejects the framing.
The objector suggests two impossible alternatives; the author denies the premise.
2
Understand the author's definition of 'indication'
The author defines 'ifāda' and 'dalāla' as a conditional relationship: whenever the proof exists, the conclusion exists; whenever the proof is considered, the conclusion is known. This refutes the objector's implications.
This is the key move – the proof does not need to be continuously present.
3
Examine the counter-objection and its resolution
A further objection is raised: the knowledge that inference does not produce knowledge would itself be either theoretical (circular) or necessary (admitted). The author responds by showing that the objection is self-defeating.
If the objection is valid, it proves the author's point; if not, it's irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصور منتف في الحقائق الإلهية - قلنا ممنوع . السادس : لو صح الاستدلال على الصانع بدليل فموجبه : إما ثبوت الصانع فيلزم انتفاؤه على تقدير انتفائه ، وإما العلم به فلا يكون دليلا عند عدم النظر فيه قلنا لا نعني بإفادته ودلالته إلا كونه بحيث متى وجد وجد المدلول ومتى نظر فيه علم المدلول فلا يلزم من انتفائه انتفاؤه، ولا من عدم النظر فيه انتفاء الحيثية، وأورد على الكل أن العلم بأن النظر لا يفيد العلم إن كان نظرياً استفيد منها فيناقض، وإن كان ضرورياً نبه عليه لم يقع فيه خلاف أكثر العقلاء . فإن قيل : عارضنا الفاسد بالفاسد قلنا : إن أفادت الفساد ثبت المطلوب وإلا لغت .
The concept is negated in divine realities - we said: it is prevented. The sixth: If inference to the Creator (al-Ṣāniʿ) by a proof were valid, its implication would be either the affirmation of the Creator, so its negation would follow upon the supposition of its negation, or knowledge of Him, so it would not be a proof when there is no consideration (naẓar) in it. We said: We do not mean by its beneficial indication (ifāda and dalāla) except that it is such that whenever it exists, the indicated exists, and whenever one considers it, the indicated is known. So its negation does not necessitate His negation, nor does the absence of consideration in it negate the aspect (al-ḥaythiyya). And it was objected to all that the knowledge that inference does not yield knowledge, if it is theoretical (naẓarī), is derived from it, so it is contradictory, and if it is necessary (ḍarūrī), it was pointed out, and most rational people have not disagreed about it. So if it is said: We countered the corrupt with the corrupt, we said: If it indicates corruption, the desired is established, otherwise it is void.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the sixth objection against the proof for the Creator (al-Ṣāniʿ). The objector argues that if inference to the Creator were valid, its implication would be either the existence of the Creator (which would entail His non-existence if the inference were absent) or knowledge of Him (which would not be a proof without considering it). The author refutes this by clarifying the meaning of 'indication' (ifāda and dalāla): it merely means that whenever the proof exists, the conclusion follows, and whenever one considers it, the conclusion is known. The negation of the proof does not negate the Creator, nor does failure to consider it negate the proof's indicative aspect. Then a further objection is raised: the knowledge that inference does not yield knowledge would itself be either theoretical (leading to circularity) or necessary (which most rational people accept). The author counters that if this objection is valid, it establishes the desired point; otherwise it is void. The argument shows the coherence of inferential knowledge in theology.

Key Terms
صانع
إفادة
دلالة
نظر
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A valid proof must always be present or always produce knowledge.
Correction: A proof's indication is conditional: whenever it exists, the conclusion follows; whenever considered, it is known. Its absence does not disprove the conclusion.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the meaning of 'ifāda' and 'dalāla' of a proof?
A) The proof necessitates the existence of the Creator at all times.
B) The proof is such that whenever it exists, the indicated exists, and whenever one considers it, the indicated is known.
C) The proof always produces knowledge whether or not it is considered.
D) The proof's implication is the negation of the Creator if the proof is absent.

Key Takeaway
A proof's indication is conditional: it shows the conclusion when present and known when considered, but its absence does not disprove the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What would it mean for your faith if you could not constantly hold the proofs for Allah's existence in your mind? Does the absence of a proof in your awareness shake your certainty?
?
The author argues that a proof's validity is not dependent on its constant consideration. How does this relieve or challenge you in your own moments of doubt or distraction?
?
If someone asks you why you believe in Allah, and you find yourself unable to recall a logical proof in that moment, do you think your faith is weakened? Why or why not?

Refuting the Circularity Objection in Proving God's Existence
The objector argues that proving God's existence is circular; answer: proof is conditional, not causal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of circularity against proving God's existence is refuted by distinguishing between causal and conditional relations.

Core Idea
A proof does not cause its conclusion; it is a conditional indicator.

This lesson addresses a sophisticated objection that threatens the very possibility of proving God's existence. The objector claims that any proof for God's existence must be circular. As you read the passage, note how the answer clarifies the nature of proof and rescues theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the two scenarios the objector presents: either the proof conveys the existence of the Maker itself or knowledge of it. See how each leads to an absurdity.
The objection is designed to show that any proof for God's existence is impossible.
2
Identify the key distinction in the answer
The answer distinguishes between a proof being a cause and being a conditional indicator. A proof is such that when it exists, the indicated exists, but it does not bring it into existence.
This distinction breaks the circularity.
3
Reflect on the importance
Consider why this refutation is crucial for the whole project of kalam: without it, theological reasoning would be impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: أن العلم بوجود الواجب هو الأساس في الإلهيات، ولا يمكن اكتسابه بالنظر، لأنه يستدعي دليلاً يفيد أمراً ويدل عليه، وذلك إما نفس ثبوت الصانع أو العلم به، وإلا لما كان دليلا عليه، فإن كان الأول لزم من انتفائه انتفاؤه ضرورة انتفاء المفاد بانتفاء المفيد وإن كان الثاني لزم من عدم النظر في الدليل ألا يكون دليلاً لأن هذا وصف إضافي لا يفرض إلا بالإضافة إلى المدلول الذي فرضناه العلم . وهو منتفي عند عدم النظر وأجيب بأنا لا نعني بكون الدليل مفيداً لشيء وموجباً له أن يوجده ويحصله على ما هو شأن العلل، بل إنه بحيث متى وجد وجد ذلك الشيء ومتى نظر فيه علم ذلك الشيء. وحاصله أن وجوده مستلزم لثبوته ، والنظر فيه مستلزم للعلم به، ومعلوم أن انتفاء الملزوم لا يوجب انتفاء اللازم، وأن عدم النظر فيه لا ينافي كونه بحيث متى نظر فيه علم المدلول.
The sixth: Knowledge of the existence of the Necessary Being (al-wajib) is the foundation in divine matters, and it cannot be acquired by consideration, because it requires a proof that conveys something and indicates it, and that is either the very existence of the Maker or knowledge of it; otherwise it would not be a proof of it. If the first, then from its non-existence follows its non-existence, necessarily from the non-existence of the conveyed by the non-existence of the conveyer. If the second, then from the absence of consideration in the proof, it would not be a proof, because this is a relational attribute that is only conceived relative to the indicated, which we assumed to be knowledge. It is nonexistent when there is no consideration. And it was answered: We do not mean by the proof's being useful for something and causing it that it brings it into existence as is the case with causes, but rather that it is such that whenever it exists, that thing exists, and whenever one considers it, he knows that thing. Its conclusion is that its existence entails its affirmation, and consideration of it entails knowledge of it. It is known that the non-existence of the entailed does not necessitate the non-existence of the entailer, and that the absence of consideration of it does not negate it being such that whenever it is considered, the indicated is known.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents a sixth objection concerning knowledge of God's existence, which is the foundation of all divine sciences. The objector argues that such knowledge cannot be acquired through nazar because it requires a proof (dalil) that either conveys the very existence of the Maker or conveys knowledge of Him. If the proof conveys existence itself, then the existence of the Maker would depend on the proof, which is absurd. If it conveys knowledge, then the proof's being a proof depends on being considered, leading to a circularity. The answer clarifies the nature of proof: the proof is not a cause that brings the indicated into existence; rather, it is a conditional relationship: whenever the proof exists, the indicated exists, and whenever one considers the proof, one knows the indicated. The proof's existence is separate from its being considered. Non-existence of the consequence does not imply non-existence of the condition, and absence of consideration does not negate the proof's conditional validity. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
العلم بوجود الواجب
الدليل
الثبوت
الملزوم/اللازم
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A proof must bring its conclusion into existence like a cause.
Correction: The answer shows that a proof is conditional: when it exists, the conclusion exists, but it does not cause it.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the sixth objection, what does it mean for a proof to be 'useful' (mufid)?
A) It brings the indicated into existence
B) It is such that when it exists, the indicated exists and when considered, the indicated is known
C) It only works if it is considered
D) It is the same as the indicated

Key Takeaway
The objection is refuted by clarifying that a proof is not a cause but a conditional: its existence entails the existence of the indicated, and considering it entails knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided seeking certainty in your faith because you feared your reasoning was incomplete or circular? How does this passage's distinction between cause and condition free you from that fear, and what step will you take this week to actively engage your intellect in knowing God?
?
If you truly believed that proofs for God's existence are conditionals that remain true even when you are not thinking about them, how would that change your response when doubts arise? Name one specific doubt you will face using this truth.
?
The objector claimed that requiring a proof for God's existence leads to an infinite regress or circularity. Do you see any area of your life where you demand absolute certainty before you act, and how does this lesson teach you to trust the conditional validity of signs from Allah?

The Sixth Objection: Circularity in Proof of the Necessary Being
Al-Sanusi presents the sixth objection: knowledge of the Necessary Being cannot be acquired through proof due to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection to the possibility of acquiring knowledge of God's existence through reasoning.

Core Idea
Proof for God cannot stand without presupposing what it tries to prove.

One of the most persistent objections in kalam is the claim that any proof for God's existence is inherently circular. The sixth objection in this passage presents that challenge in a precise logical form. Before we can assess the strength of the response, we must first grasp the objection itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: the objection sets up a dilemma.
The text is cut off; we only have the objection, not the answer.
2
Identify the two possibilities for what a proof conveys
The objection states that a proof must convey either the existence of the Maker or knowledge of Him. Find these two branches in the text.
These correspond to 'نفس ثبوت الصانع' and 'العلم به'.
3
Understand why each leads to a problem
For the first branch, the objection says that if the proof conveys existence, then absence of existence would entail absence of proof (circular). For the second, if it conveys knowledge, that knowledge would itself require a proof (infinite regress). Work through these implications.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on why this objection challenges the very possibility of rational theology. How would a theologian respond? (We will see the answer in a later lesson.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: أن العلم بوجود الواجب هو الأساس في الإلهيات، ولا يمكن اكتسابه بالنظر، لأنه يستدعي دليلاً يفيد أمراً ويدل عليه، وذلك إما نفس ثبوت الصانع أو العلم به، وإلا لما كان دليلاً عليه فإن كان الأول لزم من انتفائه انتفاؤه ضرورة انتفاء المفاد بانتفاء المفيد، وإن كان الثاني لزم من عدم النظر في
Sixth: That knowledge of the existence of the Necessary Being is the foundation in divine matters, and it cannot be acquired through reasoning, because it requires a proof that conveys something and indicates it. That [something] is either the very existence of the Maker or knowledge of Him; otherwise it would not be a proof for Him. If it is the first, then from its negation would follow its negation necessarily because of the negation of the conveyed due to the negation of the conveyer. And if it is the second, then from the absence of reasoning in …
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection (السادس) against the possibility of acquiring knowledge of the existence of the Necessary Being (الواجب) through reasoning. The objection states that such knowledge is foundational for divine matters, so it cannot be acquired by reasoning because any proof (دليل) for it would require that either the very existence of the Maker (نفس ثبوت الصانع) or knowledge of Him (العلم به) be already present as what is conveyed (مفاد) or indicated. If the proof conveys the existence itself, then the absence of that existence would entail the absence of the proof (since the conveyed is absent when the conveyer is absent). If the proof conveys prior knowledge of Him, then to avoid circularity, one would need reasoning to obtain that knowledge, which would lead to an infinite regress or presupposition. The objection is cut off before the answer is given; the argument presented is a classic circularity objection against the possibility of a proof for God's existence.

Key Terms
الواجب
ثبوت الصانع
الدليل
المفاد
المفيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies that God's existence can be known at all.
Correction: The objection only challenges a specific method (discursive proof) and does not deny other forms of knowledge (e.g., innate knowledge, revelation).

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the sixth objection?
A) Reasoning requires prior knowledge, which is impossible.
B) Any proof for God's existence either presupposes His existence or prior knowledge of Him, leading to circularity.
C) The Necessary Being is simple and cannot be known.
D) Reasoning can only yield probabilities, not certainty.

Key Takeaway
The sixth objection argues that any proof for God's existence leads to circularity or infinite regress, challenging the possibility of rational knowledge of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your belief in God rest on a chain of reasoning that, if examined, might assume what it tries to prove? What would remain of your faith if that chain were broken?
?
The objection suggests that any proof for God presupposes either His existence or prior knowledge of Him. In your own heart, do you find that you already know God in a way that precedes arguments? What is the source of that knowledge, and how do you nurture it?
?
Have you ever tried to convince someone of God's existence using reason, only to realize that your argument assumed the very point you were trying to prove? How did that experience shape your understanding of faith and proof—and what might it require you to change about how you share your belief?

The Sixth Objection and the Nature of Proof
Sanusi presents the sixth objection and gives the initial answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection against acquiring knowledge of God through reasoning and the key distinction between a proof (dalil) and a cause (illa).

Core Idea
A proof indicates its object; it does not create it.

The sixth objection argues that knowledge of the Necessary Being cannot be acquired through theoretical reasoning because any proof would either be the existence itself or knowledge of it, leading to circularity. In response, the author clarifies that a proof does not bring about its object like a cause but merely indicates it, and the relationship is based on existence and reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'dalil' and 'madlul'.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objector presents two possibilities for what a proof brings: either the existence of the Creator or knowledge of Him. Find these two horns and see how each leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand the author's answer
The author distinguishes a proof (dalil) from a cause (illa). A proof does not create its object; it indicates it. Grasp this distinction and how it resolves the dilemma.
Key phrase: 'la na'ni... annahu yujiduhu wa-yuhassiluhu'
4
Reflect on the additional argument about fallacy
The author uses an argument from fallacy: if reflecting on a fallacy necessarily led to ignorance, then verifiers would be more likely to be ignorant, but they are not. This shows that fallacy does not have the same necessity as a proof.
This connects to the learning objective about the nature of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: أن العلم بوجود الواجب هو الأساس في الإلهيات، ولا يمكن اكتسابه بالنظر، لأنه يستدعي دليلاً يفيد أمراً ويدل عليه، وذلك إما نفس ثبوت الصانع أو العلم به، وإلا لما كان دليلاً عليه فإن كان الأول لزم من انتفائه انتفاؤه ضرورة انتفاء المفاد بانتفاء المفيد، وإن كان الثاني لزم من عدم النظر في الدليل أن لا يكون دليلاً، لأن هذا وصف إضافي لا يعرض إلا بالإضافة إلى المدلول الذي فرضناه العلم وهو منتف عند عدم النظر، وأجيب بأنا لا نعني بكون الدليل مفيداً بشيء وموجباً له أنه يوجده ويحصله على ما هو شأن العلل بل بأنه بحيث متى وجد وجد ذلك الشيء ومتى نظر فيه علم ذلك بظهور الغلط ولكان المحققون بل المعصومون عن الخطأ أولى بأن يستلزم نظرهم في الشبهة الجهل، بناء على أنهم أحق الاطلاع على وجه الدلالة فيها، وهذا بخلاف الدليل فإن له صفة ووجه دلالة في ذاته وهو مناط استلزامه المطلوب عند حصول الشرائط، وأما اللزوم العائد إلى اعتقاد الناظر في بعض الصور كما إذا اعتقد حقية المقدمات في المثال المذكور فلا نزاع فيه واعترض الإمام بأن عدم حصول الجهل للمحق الناظر في شبهة المبطل يجوز أن يكون بناء على عدم اطلاعه على ما فيها من جهة الاستلزام أو عدم اعتقاده حقية المقدمات كما أن نظر المبطل في دليل المحق لا يستلزم العلم بذلك، وما ذكر من كون المحق أولى بالاطلاع إنما هو فيما يفيد الحق والعلم لا الباطل. والجهل.
Sixth: Knowledge of the existence of the Necessary Being is the foundation in divine matters, and it cannot be acquired through theoretical reasoning, because it requires a proof (dalil) that benefits a matter and indicates it, and that is either the very existence of the Creator or knowledge of it; otherwise it would not be a proof of it. If the first, then its non-existence would entail its non-existence necessarily due to the non-existence of the benefit by the non-existence of the benefiter. If the second, then absence of reflection on the proof would entail that it not be a proof, because this is a relational attribute that only occurs in relation to the indicated thing which we assumed to be knowledge, and it is absent when there is no reflection. And it was answered: We do not mean by the proof benefiting something and causing it that it brings it into existence and produces it in the manner of causes, but rather that it is such that whenever it exists, that thing exists, and whenever one reflects on it, one knows it. And by this the error is evident: if reflection on fallacy necessitates ignorance, then the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) and even those protected from error would be more deserving that their reflection on fallacy would necessitate ignorance, based on their being more entitled to awareness of the aspect of indication in it; but this is not the case. This is contrary to the proof (dalil), for it has an attribute and an aspect of indication in itself, and it is the basis of its entailing the sought thing when conditions are met. As for the necessity that goes back to the belief of the observer in some cases, as when he believes the truth of the premises in the mentioned example, there is no dispute about it. And Imam [al-Razi] objected: The absence of ignorance for the verifier who reflects on a fallacy of the falsifier may be due to his lack of awareness of what is in it of the aspect of entailment or his lack of belief in the truth of the premises, just as the reflection of the falsifier on the proof of the verifier does not necessitate knowledge of it. And what was mentioned about the verifier being more entitled to awareness is only concerning what brings truth and knowledge, not falsehood and ignorance.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection against the possibility of acquiring knowledge of God's existence through theoretical reasoning. The objector argues that such reasoning would require a proof (dalil) that indicates something. That proof must be either the existence of the Creator itself or knowledge of it. If it is the existence itself, then the absence of the proof would imply the absence of the thing, which is false. If it is knowledge, then without reflection the proof would not be a proof because being a proof is relational and only occurs in relation to the indicated thing (knowledge), which is absent without reflection. Thus, reasoning cannot produce knowledge of God. The answer distinguishes between a cause (illa) that brings about existence and a proof (dalil) that merely indicates. A proof does not create the thing; rather, whenever the proof exists, the thing exists, and whenever one reflects on it, one knows it. Then a further point is made: if reflection on a fallacy necessarily leads to ignorance, then the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) and even the infallible would be more likely to become ignorant when reflecting on fallacies, but this is not the case. This shows that fallacy does not necessitate ignorance in the same way that a true proof necessitates knowledge. The proof has its own intrinsic indicative aspect, while the entailment from fallacy depends on the observer's belief. Imam al-Razi then objects: the verifier's lack of ignorance when examining a fallacy might be due to his lack of awareness of the implicative aspect or his disbelief in the premises, not because the fallacy itself lacks necessity. The claim that the verifier is more entitled to awareness applies only to what brings truth and knowledge, not falsehood and ignorance. Thus, the answer is nuanced.

Key Terms
واجب
إلهيات
دليل
مدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A proof must bring about its object like a physical cause.
Correction: A proof only indicates its object; it does not create it.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the sixth objection, how does a proof (dalil) differ from a cause (illa)?
A) A proof creates its object, while a cause does not
B) A proof indicates its object without creating it, while a cause brings it into existence
C) A proof is only for necessary matters, while a cause is for contingent matters
D) A proof requires belief, while a cause does not

Key Takeaway
A proof (dalil) does not create its object but indicates it, which resolves the circularity objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself doubting God's existence, do you sometimes feel that your reasoning is useless because it can't 'create' certainty? How does this lesson's distinction between a proof (dalil) and a cause (illa) challenge that feeling? What would it take for you to trust that the proof indicates enough for knowledge?
?
The objector argued that if a proof brought about knowledge, then without reflection it wouldn't be a proof. Have you been avoiding serious reflection on the signs of God, perhaps because you think that if the proof were real, you would feel certainty without effort? What has this avoidance cost you? What will you do today to reflect on one clear proof of God's existence?
?
This lesson teaches that a proof has an intrinsic indicative aspect, independent of your belief. Are you living as though the proofs of God's existence are only valid when you 'feel' they are? If the proof is objectively valid, what does that demand from you right now, even if your current state is doubt or distraction?

Imam al-Razi's Objection and Final Clarification
Imam al-Razi objects to the answer and the author provides a final clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's objection to the answer and the final clarification regarding the difference between true proofs and fallacies.

Core Idea
The verifier's immunity from ignorance when examining fallacies is due to his awareness or belief, not the fallacy's intrinsic necessity.

After the initial answer, Imam al-Razi raises an objection: why does the verifier not become ignorant when reflecting on a fallacy? The answer given earlier is nuanced, and al-Razi suggests it may be due to the verifier's lack of awareness or disbelief in premises. The author then clarifies that the earlier point about the verifier being more entitled to awareness applies only to truth, not falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read Imam al-Razi's objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the structure of al-Razi's argument.
Note the parallel he draws with the falsifier.
2
Identify the two possible reasons for the verifier's ignorance
Al-Razi gives two explanations: lack of awareness of the implicative aspect, or disbelief in the premises. Understand both.
3
Grasp the final clarification
The author concludes that the earlier point about the verifier being more entitled to awareness is limited to what brings truth and knowledge, not falsehood and ignorance.
This refines the answer and acknowledges the role of the observer's subjective state.

السادس: أن العلم بوجود الواجب هو الأساس في الإلهيات، ولا يمكن اكتسابه بالنظر، لأنه يستدعي دليلاً يفيد أمراً ويدل عليه، وذلك إما نفس ثبوت الصانع أو العلم به، وإلا لما كان دليلاً عليه فإن كان الأول لزم من انتفائه انتفاؤه ضرورة انتفاء المفاد بانتفاء المفيد، وإن كان الثاني لزم من عدم النظر في الدليل أن لا يكون دليلاً، لأن هذا وصف إضافي لا يعرض إلا بالإضافة إلى المدلول الذي فرضناه العلم وهو منتف عند عدم النظر، وأجيب بأنا لا نعني بكون الدليل مفيداً بشيء وموجباً له أنه يوجده ويحصله على ما هو شأن العلل بل بأنه بحيث متى وجد وجد ذلك الشيء ومتى نظر فيه علم ذلك بظهور الغلط ولكان المحققون بل المعصومون عن الخطأ أولى بأن يستلزم نظرهم في الشبهة الجهل، بناء على أنهم أحق الاطلاع على وجه الدلالة فيها، وهذا بخلاف الدليل فإن له صفة ووجه دلالة في ذاته وهو مناط استلزامه المطلوب عند حصول الشرائط، وأما اللزوم العائد إلى اعتقاد الناظر في بعض الصور كما إذا اعتقد حقية المقدمات في المثال المذكور فلا نزاع فيه واعترض الإمام بأن عدم حصول الجهل للمحق الناظر في شبهة المبطل يجوز أن يكون بناء على عدم اطلاعه على ما فيها من جهة الاستلزام أو عدم اعتقاده حقية المقدمات كما أن نظر المبطل في دليل المحق لا يستلزم العلم بذلك، وما ذكر من كون المحق أولى بالاطلاع إنما هو فيما يفيد الحق والعلم لا الباطل. والجهل.
Sixth: Knowledge of the existence of the Necessary Being is the foundation in divine matters, and it cannot be acquired through theoretical reasoning, because it requires a proof (dalil) that benefits a matter and indicates it, and that is either the very existence of the Creator or knowledge of it; otherwise it would not be a proof of it. If the first, then its non-existence would entail its non-existence necessarily due to the non-existence of the benefit by the non-existence of the benefiter. If the second, then absence of reflection on the proof would entail that it not be a proof, because this is a relational attribute that only occurs in relation to the indicated thing which we assumed to be knowledge, and it is absent when there is no reflection. And it was answered: We do not mean by the proof benefiting something and causing it that it brings it into existence and produces it in the manner of causes, but rather that it is such that whenever it exists, that thing exists, and whenever one reflects on it, one knows it. And by this the error is evident: if reflection on fallacy necessitates ignorance, then the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) and even those protected from error would be more deserving that their reflection on fallacy would necessitate ignorance, based on their being more entitled to awareness of the aspect of indication in it; but this is not the case. This is contrary to the proof (dalil), for it has an attribute and an aspect of indication in itself, and it is the basis of its entailing the sought thing when conditions are met. As for the necessity that goes back to the belief of the observer in some cases, as when he believes the truth of the premises in the mentioned example, there is no dispute about it. And Imam [al-Razi] objected: The absence of ignorance for the verifier who reflects on a fallacy of the falsifier may be due to his lack of awareness of what is in it of the aspect of entailment or his lack of belief in the truth of the premises, just as the reflection of the falsifier on the proof of the verifier does not necessitate knowledge of it. And what was mentioned about the verifier being more entitled to awareness is only concerning what brings truth and knowledge, not falsehood and ignorance.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection against the possibility of acquiring knowledge of God's existence through theoretical reasoning. The objector argues that such reasoning would require a proof (dalil) that indicates something. That proof must be either the existence of the Creator itself or knowledge of it. If it is the existence itself, then the absence of the proof would imply the absence of the thing, which is false. If it is knowledge, then without reflection the proof would not be a proof because being a proof is relational and only occurs in relation to the indicated thing (knowledge), which is absent without reflection. Thus, reasoning cannot produce knowledge of God. The answer distinguishes between a cause (illa) that brings about existence and a proof (dalil) that merely indicates. A proof does not create the thing; rather, whenever the proof exists, the thing exists, and whenever one reflects on it, one knows it. Then a further point is made: if reflection on a fallacy necessarily leads to ignorance, then the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) and even the infallible would be more likely to become ignorant when reflecting on fallacies, but this is not the case. This shows that fallacy does not necessitate ignorance in the same way that a true proof necessitates knowledge. The proof has its own intrinsic indicative aspect, while the entailment from fallacy depends on the observer's belief. Imam al-Razi then objects: the verifier's lack of ignorance when examining a fallacy might be due to his lack of awareness of the implicative aspect or his disbelief in the premises, not because the fallacy itself lacks necessity. The claim that the verifier is more entitled to awareness applies only to what brings truth and knowledge, not falsehood and ignorance. Thus, the answer is nuanced.

Critique of Definitions of Existence
The author critiques common definitions of existence, showing their circularity and lack of explanatory power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical attempts to define existence (al-wujud) are circular or fail to be clearer, and how the concept of existence is presupposed by any such definition.

Core Idea
Existence is too fundamental to be defined — any definition already presupposes it.

This passage from al-Sanusi's Sharh al-Maqasid critiques attempts to define existence (al-wujud). Various definitions are examined, such as 'that which is established' or 'the fixed-in-essence', and they are shown to be either circular or more obscure than the term they aim to define. The author demonstrates that existence is a primary concept that cannot be captured by simpler terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the author's critical tone and the variety of definitions he addresses.
Do not try to analyze deeply yet; just absorb the flow.
2
Identify the circularity argument
The author first points out that defining existence as 'that which is established' or 'that which is possible' involves circularity: you already need to understand existence to grasp those terms. Find this part in the passage.
Circularity (al-dawr) is a logical flaw in definition.
3
Consider the nominal definition objection
Even if the definitions are treated as nominal (explaining the word), they are not clearer than 'existence' itself. Locate the author's reasoning on this point.
A nominal definition should at least be easier to understand than the defined term.
4
Analyze the term 'al-thabit al-'ayn'
The passage discusses whether 'the fixed-in-essence' (al-thabit al-'ayn) is a valid definition. See how the author argues that 'fixed' simply means 'existent', and the addition of 'essence' does not help.
Some earlier scholars used this phrase, but it does not escape circularity.
5
Reflect on alternative definitions
The author mentions al-Farabi's definition of existence as 'potentiality for action and passion'. Note that this definition is also considered and implicitly found lacking, as it is not a clear improvement.
The broader lesson is that existence resists definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدور ظاهر، إذ لا يعقل معنى الذي ثبت والذي أمكن ونحو ذلك إلا بعد تعقل معنى الحصول في الأعيان أو الأذهان، ولو سلم فلا خفاء في أن معنى الوجود أوضح عند العقل من معاني هذه (۱) العبارات، وقد يقرر الدور بأن الموصوف المقدر لهذه الصفات أعني الذي يثبت والذي يمكن والذي ينقسم هو الوجود لا غير، لأن غيره إما الموجود أو العدم (۲) أو المعدوم ، ولا شيء منها يصدق على الوجود وهو ضعيف، لأن المفهومات لا تنحصر فيما ذكر فيجوز أن يقدر مثل المعنى والأمر والشيء مما يصدق على الوجود . (وهو) ضعيف لأن المفهومات لا تنحصر فيما ذكر فيجوز أن يقدر مثل المعنى والأمر والشيء مما يصدق على الوجود وغيره) (۳) المقصود من وإن قصد كونه تعريفاً اسمياً فلا خفاء في أنه ليس أوضح دلالة على لفظ الوجود بل أخفى فلا يصلح تعريفاً (٤) اسمياً، كما لا يصلح رسمياً، على أن كلا منها صادق على الموجود، وبعضها على أعيان الموجودات، وقد يتكلف لعدم صدق الثابت العين (٥) على الموجودات بأن معناه الثابت عينه أي نفسه من حيث هي لا باعتبار أمر آخر بخلاف الموجود فإنه ثابت من حيث اتصافه بالوجود، فالثابت أعم من أن يكون ثابتاً بنفسه وهو الوجود، أو بالوجود وهو الموجودات، وأنت خبير بأنه لا دلالة للفظ عينه على هذا المعنى، ولا يعقل من الثابت إلا ماله الثبوت، وهو معنى الموجود، وكون هذه التعريفات للوجود هو ظاهر كلام التجريد والمباحث المشرقية وفي كلام المتقدمين أن الموجود هو الثابت العين والمعدوم هو المنفي العين، وكأن زيادة لفظ العين لدفع توهم أن يراد الثابت لشيء والمنفي عن شيء فإن ذلك معنى المحمول لا الموجود، وفي كلام الفارابي (١) أن الوجود إمكان الفعل والانفعال، والموجود ما أمكنه الفعل والانفعال.
The circularity is apparent, for the meaning of 'that which is established' and 'that which is possible' and the like cannot be conceived except after conceiving the meaning of occurrence in external entities or minds. And if it is granted, there is no hiding that the meaning of existence is clearer to the intellect than the meanings of these expressions. The circularity is sometimes stated as follows: the described thing intended for these attributes—namely, that which is established, that which is possible, and that which is divisible—is existence alone, because other than it is either the existent or non-existence or the nonexistent, and none of these are true of existence. But this is weak, because concepts are not limited to what was mentioned, so it is possible to conceive such as 'meaning', 'affair', and 'thing' which are true of existence. (And it is weak because concepts are not limited to what was mentioned, so it is possible to conceive such as 'meaning', 'affair', and 'thing' which are true of existence and other than it.) The purpose of... And if it is intended as a nominal definition, there is no hiding that it is not clearer in indication of the word 'existence' but rather more obscure, so it is not suitable as a nominal definition, just as it is not suitable as a real definition. Moreover, each of them is true of the existent, and some of them are true of the essences of existents. One may strain to deny that 'the fixed-in-essence' is true of existents, claiming that its meaning is 'that which is fixed in its essence', i.e., itself as such, not by virtue of another, unlike the existent which is fixed by virtue of its being described with existence. Thus, 'fixed' is more general than being fixed by itself, which is existence, or by existence, which are existents. But you are aware that the word 'essence' has no indication of this meaning, and nothing is understood from 'fixed' except that which has fixity, which is the meaning of 'existent'. That these definitions are for existence is apparent from the words of al-Tajrid and al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, and in the words of the early scholars: 'the existent is the fixed-in-essence, and the nonexistent is the negated-in-essence.' It is as if the addition of the word 'essence' is to prevent the misunderstanding that what is intended is 'fixed for something' and 'negated for something'—for that is the meaning of a predicate, not of the existent. And in the words of al-Farabi: 'existence is the possibility of acting and being acted upon, and the existent is that which can act and be acted upon.'
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage critiques various attempts to define existence (al-wujud). The author begins by pointing out the circularity (al-dawr) in defining existence through terms like 'that which is established' (al-thabit) or 'that which is possible' (al-mumkin), because understanding those terms already presupposes understanding existence itself. Even if one grants the possibility, the meaning of existence is clearer than those terms. The author then examines a specific formulation of the circularity argument: that the subject described by these attributes is existence alone, since alternatives like 'the existent', 'non-existence', or 'the nonexistent' are not true of existence. But this is weak because concepts are not limited to those; one could use 'meaning' (al-ma'na), 'affair' (al-amr), or 'thing' (al-shay') which apply to existence. Next, the author considers if these are intended as nominal definitions. They are not clearer than 'existence' but rather more obscure, so they fail as nominal definitions too. The author then discusses the phrase 'al-thabit al-'ayn' (the fixed-in-essence) used by earlier scholars. Some argue it means self-subsistent, distinct from 'existent' which is fixed by virtue of existence. But the word 'ayn' does not convey this, and 'fixed' only means having fixity, which is synonymous with 'existent'. The passage concludes by noting that some philosophers, like al-Farabi, defined existence as potentiality for action and passion.

الدور (al-dawr)
الثابت العين (al-thabit al-'ayn)
الموجود (al-mawjud)
المعدوم (al-ma'dum)
التعريف (al-ta'rif)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence can be captured by a non-circular definition using terms like 'established' or 'possible'.
Correction: The passage shows such attempts either presuppose existence or are less clear, failing both as real and nominal definitions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Existence is a self-evident concept that cannot be defined without circularity or obscurity; any attempt at definition fails.

The First Proof for Self-Evidence of Existence
The author presents the first proof and responds to an objection about circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the self-evidence of the concept of existence and the dialectical exchange about whether it involves circularity.

Core Idea
Proving self-evidence requires careful logic to avoid circular reasoning.

This lesson unpacks the first proof offered by the author: that the self-evident judgment of the mutual contradiction between existence and non-existence already presupposes the concept of existence. But is that circular? The text explores objections and refinements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the flow.
2
Identify the proof and objection
Find where the proof is stated and where the objection begins. Notice the dialectical structure: claim, objection, response.
The objection challenges the notion of 'absolute self-evidence'.
3
Examine the counter-objection
The author presents a possible counter-argument about the whole depending on parts, then refutes it. Understand why the dependence is necessary.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why proving self-evidence is not trivial and why the author engages in this dialectic.
This shows the rigor of kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصديق ببداهة تصور الوجود قال : واستدل بوجوه الأول : أن التصديق البديهي بتنافي الوجود والعدم يتوقف على تصوره(۲)، ورد بأنه أريد البداهة مطلقاً(۳) بمعنى عدم التوقف على الكسب أصلاً فممنوع، بل مصادرة أو بداهة الحكم فغير مفيد إذ لا يستلزم تصور الحقيقة ولا ينافي اكتسابه لا يقال بداهة الكل وان توقفت على بداهة الأجزاء لكن العلم ببداهته لا يتوقف على العلم ببداهتها بل يستتبعه فلا مضادرة) لأنا نقول توقف العلم ببداهة الكل على العلم ببداهة الجزء ضروري كتوقف الكل على الجزء إذ بداهة الجزء جزء بداهة الكل، والعلم بالكل إما نفس العلم بالأجزاء أو حاصل به) . الثاني : : أنه معلوم يمتنع اكتسابه إما بالحد (٥) فلبساطته إذ لو تركب فأما من الموجودات فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، ومساواة الجزء للكل في ماهيته، أو من غيرها فلا بد أن يحصل عند الاجتماع أمر زائد يكون هو الموجود، لئلا
The affirmation of the self-evidence of the conceptualization of existence. He said: And he adduced proofs: The first: that the self-evident affirmation of the mutual contradiction of existence and non-existence depends on its conceptualization. It is replied: if what is meant by self-evidence is absolute self-evidence in the sense of not depending on acquisition at all, then it is denied; rather it is a petitio principii. Or if it is the self-evidence of the judgment, this is not useful because it does not necessitate the conceptualization of the reality, nor does it preclude its acquisition. It cannot be said: the self-evidence of the whole, even if it depends on the self-evidence of the parts, but the knowledge of its self-evidence does not depend on the knowledge of their self-evidence; rather it is entailed, so there is no circularity. For we say: the dependence of the knowledge of the self-evidence of the whole on the knowledge of the self-evidence of the part is necessary, just as the whole depends on the part, for the self-evidence of the part is part of the self-evidence of the whole, and knowledge of the whole is either identical to knowledge of the parts or derived from it. The second: that it is known, and its acquisition is impossible either by definition (al-hadd) because of its simplicity—for if it were composite, then either from existents, leading to the priority of a thing over itself and the equality of the part with the whole in its quiddity, or from other things, then at their conjunction an additional thing must arise which is the existent, lest...
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the self-evident (badihiyya) nature of the concept of existence (tasawwur al-wujud). The first proof argues that the self-evident judgment of the mutual contradiction between existence and non-existence requires prior conceptualization of existence, but the author then discusses an objection: if the self-evidence claimed is absolute (i.e., not dependent on any acquisition), then the proof is circular; if it is the self-evidence of the judgment alone, that does not entail the self-evidence of the concept. The author also addresses a counter-argument about the whole depending on parts, affirming that knowledge of the whole's self-evidence does depend on knowledge of the parts' self-evidence. The second proof argues that existence cannot be acquired through definition (al-hadd) because it is simple (basit). If it were composite, its components would either be from existents (leading to circularity) or from non-existents (which would require an additional thing to arise—the existent—thus still presupposing existence). The passage cuts off mid-argument at 'lest...' (لئلا).

Key Terms
بداهة (badāha)
تصور (taṣawwur)
الوجود (al-wujūd)
التصديق (al-taṣdīq)
مصادرة (muṣādara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the self-evidence of existence is so obvious that no proof is needed.
Correction: The author shows that even self-evident concepts require careful argument to establish their self-evidence without circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what would be the problem if 'self-evidence' means absolute self-evidence?
A) It would make the proof tautological.
B) It would be too strong.
C) It would require no proof at all.
D) It would be impossible to define.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for the self-evidence of existence must navigate the charge of circularity by carefully distinguishing between absolute self-evidence and self-evidence of the judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What belief about God do you hold as so obvious that you have never questioned its logical foundation? After this lesson, are you willing to examine whether that belief is truly self-evident or merely assumed?
?
The author argues that knowledge of the whole depends on knowledge of its parts. Consider your understanding of Tawheed: is it built on self-evident parts that you have verified, or have you accepted the whole without examining the parts? What one part will you analyze today?
?
This lesson exposes the danger of circular reasoning in matters of faith. When you defend your beliefs, do you ever realize you are assuming what you set out to prove? Pledge to identify one such circularity in your own reasoning and correct it this week.

The Second Proof: Simplicity Prevents Definition
The author presents the second proof that existence cannot be defined due to simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of existence cannot be acquired through definition due to its simplicity, and what consequences arise if we attempt to compose it.

Core Idea
Existence is so simple that any attempt to define it fails.

The second proof takes a different approach: it argues that the concept of existence is simple, therefore it cannot be defined. Definitions require composition, and any composition of existence leads to circularity or an infinite regress. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof.
2
Understand the claim: simplicity prevents definition
The author states that existence cannot be acquired by definition because it is simple. Ask: why does simplicity block definition?
A definition gives the genus and differentia, which are parts.
3
Analyze the hypothetical composition
The author imagines if existence were composite, its parts would either be existents or non-existents. Follow the reasoning for each case.
Both lead to absurdities.
4
Note the incomplete end
The passage cuts off mid-sentence. Consider what the argument would need to complete: the conclusion that existence is indeed simple and self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصديق ببداهة تصور الوجود قال : واستدل بوجوه الأول : أن التصديق البديهي بتنافي الوجود والعدم يتوقف على تصوره(۲)، ورد بأنه أريد البداهة مطلقاً(۳) بمعنى عدم التوقف على الكسب أصلاً فممنوع، بل مصادرة أو بداهة الحكم فغير مفيد إذ لا يستلزم تصور الحقيقة ولا ينافي اكتسابه لا يقال بداهة الكل وان توقفت على بداهة الأجزاء لكن العلم ببداهته لا يتوقف على العلم ببداهتها بل يستتبعه فلا مضادرة) لأنا نقول توقف العلم ببداهة الكل على العلم ببداهة الجزء ضروري كتوقف الكل على الجزء إذ بداهة الجزء جزء بداهة الكل، والعلم بالكل إما نفس العلم بالأجزاء أو حاصل به) . الثاني : : أنه معلوم يمتنع اكتسابه إما بالحد (٥) فلبساطته إذ لو تركب فأما من الموجودات فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، ومساواة الجزء للكل في ماهيته، أو من غيرها فلا بد أن يحصل عند الاجتماع أمر زائد يكون هو الموجود، لئلا
The affirmation of the self-evidence of the conceptualization of existence. He said: And he adduced proofs: The first: that the self-evident affirmation of the mutual contradiction of existence and non-existence depends on its conceptualization. It is replied: if what is meant by self-evidence is absolute self-evidence in the sense of not depending on acquisition at all, then it is denied; rather it is a petitio principii. Or if it is the self-evidence of the judgment, this is not useful because it does not necessitate the conceptualization of the reality, nor does it preclude its acquisition. It cannot be said: the self-evidence of the whole, even if it depends on the self-evidence of the parts, but the knowledge of its self-evidence does not depend on the knowledge of their self-evidence; rather it is entailed, so there is no circularity. For we say: the dependence of the knowledge of the self-evidence of the whole on the knowledge of the self-evidence of the part is necessary, just as the whole depends on the part, for the self-evidence of the part is part of the self-evidence of the whole, and knowledge of the whole is either identical to knowledge of the parts or derived from it. The second: that it is known, and its acquisition is impossible either by definition (al-hadd) because of its simplicity—for if it were composite, then either from existents, leading to the priority of a thing over itself and the equality of the part with the whole in its quiddity, or from other things, then at their conjunction an additional thing must arise which is the existent, lest...
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the self-evident (badihiyya) nature of the concept of existence (tasawwur al-wujud). The first proof argues that the self-evident judgment of the mutual contradiction between existence and non-existence requires prior conceptualization of existence, but the author then discusses an objection: if the self-evidence claimed is absolute (i.e., not dependent on any acquisition), then the proof is circular; if it is the self-evidence of the judgment alone, that does not entail the self-evidence of the concept. The author also addresses a counter-argument about the whole depending on parts, affirming that knowledge of the whole's self-evidence does depend on knowledge of the parts' self-evidence. The second proof argues that existence cannot be acquired through definition (al-hadd) because it is simple (basit). If it were composite, its components would either be from existents (leading to circularity) or from non-existents (which would require an additional thing to arise—the existent—thus still presupposing existence). The passage cuts off mid-argument at 'lest...' (لئلا).

Key Terms
الحد (al-ḥadd)
البساطة (al-basāṭa)
التركيب (al-tarkīb)
الموجودات (al-mawjūdāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'simple' means easy; here it means not composed of parts.
Correction: Simplicity in metaphysics means lacking composition, not being easy to understand.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if existence were composed from existent parts?
A) The parts would be simpler than the whole.
B) The thing would be prior to itself and the part equal to the whole.
C) The parts would be non-existent.
D) The definition would be circular.

Key Takeaway
The second proof establishes that existence is simple, making definition impossible, which supports its self-evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is so simple that it cannot be defined, what does that reveal about your own attempts to intellectually grasp ultimate reality? Are you relying on definitions that ultimately fail? How will you seek certainty instead?
?
The proof shows that assuming composition leads to contradictions. In your own life, where have you assumed that complex definitions can capture the deepest truths? Will you now accept that some truths must be directly witnessed, not defined?
?
If existence is self-evident and known without definition, then why do you often demand logical proofs for everything before believing? Could your insistence on definition be a barrier to recognizing what is already clear? What will you do to train your heart to see the obvious?

Impossibility of Acquiring Existence Through Definition or Description
Proves existence cannot be known by definition or description and refutes composition in existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence cannot be acquired through definition or description, and the implications of composition in existence.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be defined because it is simple; any definition attempt leads to absurdities.

This passage tackles a crucial question: can we come to know existence (al-wujud) through logical definitions or descriptions? The author argues that since existence is known by its essence, any attempt to acquire knowledge of it via definition or description fails. He presents a detailed proof of why definition requires composition, which existence lacks, and why description leads to circularity. An objection (naqd) is also raised against the proof, showing its potential self-defeating nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, then the translation. Note the logical flow from the premise that existence is known by its essence to the conclusion that it cannot be acquired.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the two paths of acquisition
Identify the two methods of acquiring knowledge: by definition (hadd) and by description (rasm). The author argues both fail for existence.
3
Analyze the argument against definition
Follow the reasoning: definition is for composites, existence is not composite. If it were, its parts would lead to absurdities like self-precedence. Consider the entailment about absolute and particular existences.
The proof uses reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the argument against description
Description requires prior knowledge of the outside property, leading to circularity. Even if granted, it does not reveal the essence.
5
Consider the naqd objection
The objection shows that the same reasoning would imply no essence is composite, which is contradictory. The text cuts off before the full answer.
This is an important critical reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : إن الوجود معلوم بحقيقته وحصول العلم إما بالضرورة أو الاكتساب وطريق الاكتساب إما الحد أو الرسم، وهذا احتجاج على من يعترف بهذه المقدمات، فلهذا لم يتعرض لمنعها والوجود يمتنع اكتسابه أما بالحد فلأنه إنما يكون للمركب، والوجود ليس بمركب، وإلا فأجزاؤه إما وجودات أو غيرها فإن كانت وجودات لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه ومساواة الجزء للكل في تمام ماهيته وكلاهما محال . أما الأول فظاهر. وأما الثاني : فلأن الجزء داخل في ماهية الكل، وليس بداخل في ماهية نفسه، ومبنى اللزوم على أن الوجود المطلق الذي فرض التركيب فيه ليس خارجاً عن الوجودات الخاصة، بل إما نفس ماهيتها ليلزم الثاني، أو جزء مقوم لها ليلزم الأول، وإلا فيجوز أن تكون الأجزاء وجودات خاصة نفس الماهيات أو زائدة عليها، والمطلق خارج عنها فلا يلزم شيء من المحالين، وإن لم تكن الأجزاء وجودات فإما أن يحصل عند اجتماعهما أمر زائد يكون هو الوجود أو لا يحصل، فإن لم يحصل كان الوجود محصنا ما ليس بوجود وهو محال، وإن حصل لم يكن التركيب في الوجود الذي هو نفس ذلك الزائد العارض بل في معروضه هذا خلف وتقرير الإمام في المباحث أنه لو تركب الوجود فأجزاؤه إن كانت وجودية كان الوجود الواحد وجودات، وإن لم تكن وجودية فإن لم يحدث لها عند اجتماعها صفة الوجود كان الوجود عبارة من مجموع الأمور العدمية، وإن حدثت يكون ذلك المجموع مؤثراً في ذلك الوجود أو قابلاً له فلا يكون التركيب في نفس الوجود بل في قابله أو فاعله وأما بالرسم فلما سبق من أنه يفيد بعد العلم باختصاص الخارج بالمرسوم وهذا متوقف على العلم به وهو دور وبما عداه مفصلاً وهو محال، ولو سلم فلا يفيد معرفة الحقيقة، والجواب عن التقرير الأول لدليل امتناع تركب الوجود النقض أي لو صح بجميع مقدماته لزم أن لا يكون شيء من الماهيات مركباً لجريانه فيها بأن يقال أجزاء البيت إما بيوت وهو محال وإما غير بيوت وحينئذ إما أن يحصل عند اجتماعها أمر زائد هو البيت فلا يكون التركيب في البيت هذا خلف أولا يحصل فيكون البيت محض
The second aspect: Indeed, existence (al-wujud) is known by its essence (haqiqa). And the attainment of knowledge is either by necessity (darura) or acquisition (iktisab). The path of acquisition is either by definition (hadd) or description (rasm). This is an argument against one who acknowledges these premises, so for this reason he did not address their refutation. And existence cannot be acquired. As for by definition, because definition is only for the composite (murakkab), and existence is not composite; otherwise, its parts would be either existences (wujudat) or other than them. If they were existences, then the thing would precede itself (taqaddum al-shay' 'ala nafsihi) and the part would equal the whole in the entirety of its essence (musawat al-juz' li'l-kull fi tamam mahiyyatihi), both of which are impossible. As for the first, it is obvious. As for the second, because the part is included in the essence of the whole, and not included in its own essence. And the basis of the entailment (luzum) is that absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) which is assumed to have composition is not outside particular existences (al-wujudat al-khassa), but rather either the very essence of them (to entail the second) or a constituent part (juz' muqawwim) for them (to entail the first). Otherwise, it is possible that the parts are particular existences that are the essences themselves or additional to them, and the absolute is outside them, so neither impossibility follows. And if the parts are not existences, then either an additional thing (amr za'id) which is existence occurs upon their conjunction, or it does not occur. If it does not occur, then existence would be a compound of what is not existence (muhassan ma laysa bi-wujud), which is impossible. And if it occurs, then composition would not be in existence which is that additional accidental thing (al-za'id al-'arid), but rather in its substrate (ma'rud), which is contrary to assumption (khalaf). And the report of the Imam in al-Mabahith: If existence were composite, then its parts: if they are existential (wujudiyya), then a single existence would be multiple existences; if they are not existential, then if no attribute of existence occurs to them upon their conjunction, existence would be an expression of a collection of non-existential things (umur 'adamiyya); and if it occurs, then that collection would be an effect (mu'aththir) on that existence or a recipient (qabil) for it, so composition would not be in existence itself but in its recipient or agent. As for by description (rasm), it has preceded that it yields knowledge after knowledge of the specific property of the outside by the described, and this depends on knowledge of it, which is circular (dawr), and of what is other than it in detail, which is impossible. And even if granted, it does not yield knowledge of the essence. The answer to the first report of the proof of the impossibility of composition of existence is the objection (naqd): i.e., if it were correct with all its premises, it would follow that no essence (mahiyya) is composite, because it applies to them. For example, one says: Parts of the house (bayt) are either houses, which is impossible, or other than houses. Then either upon their conjunction an additional thing which is the house occurs, so composition is not in the house — this is contrary to assumption; or it does not occur, so the house would be pure (mahd)...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a proof and an objection-and-answer concerning existence (al-wujud). The author presents the second argument: existence is known by its essence, and knowledge is either necessary (daruri) or acquired (iktisabi). The acquired path is either by definition (hadd) or description (rasm). The argument is directed at those who accept these premises. Existence cannot be acquired by definition because definition applies only to composites, and existence is not composite. If it were composite, its parts would be either existences or non-existences. If existences, then two impossibilities follow: a thing preceding itself (taqaddum al-shay' 'ala nafsihi) and the part equaling the whole in its entire essence (musawat al-juz' li'l-kull fi tamam mahiyya). The first is obvious, the second because the part is included in the essence of the whole but not in its own essence. The author then explains the basis of this entailment: absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) assumed to have composition is not outside particular existences. It is either their very essence or a constituent part; otherwise, the parts could be particular existences themselves, and the absolute is outside them, so no impossibility. If the parts are not existences, then either an additional thing (amr za'id) which is existence occurs upon their conjunction or not. If not, existence would be composed of non-existence, impossible. If it occurs, then composition is not in existence itself but in its substrate, contrary to assumption. The author then cites the report of the Imam (likely al-Razi) in al-Mabahith: if existence were composite, its parts if existential would make a single existence multiple existences; if non-existential, then either no attribute of existence arises, making existence a collection of non-existentials, or it arises, making the collection an effect or recipient, so composition is not in existence itself. As for acquisition by description (rasm), it has been shown that it yields knowledge after knowledge of the specific property, which is circular. Even if granted, it does not yield knowledge of the essence. Then the objection (naqd) to the first report is given: if this proof were sound with all its premises, it would entail that no essence (mahiyya) is composite, because the same reasoning applies to any composite. For instance, the parts of a house are either houses (impossible) or non-houses. Then either an additional house occurs upon conjunction (so composition is not in the house itself, contrary to assumption) or not (so the house is pure non-house). Thus the proof is self-defeating.

مساواة الجزء للكل
وجود مطلق
وجودات خاصة
نقض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence can be defined by a simple definition.
Correction: Definition requires composition, which existence lacks, leading to absurdities if assumed otherwise.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection (naqd) raised against the proof that existence cannot be composite?
A) The proof uses circular reasoning.
B) The proof would imply that no essence, like a house, can be composite.
C) The proof relies on an incorrect definition of existence.
D) The proof assumes existence is known by description.

Key Takeaway
Existence is simple and cannot be acquired through definition or description; attempts lead to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even the concept of 'existence' cannot be defined or described without contradiction, how does that humble your attempts to fully grasp the reality of Allah? What do you lose when you insist on a logical definition of His existence?
?
The passage shows that any attempt to know existence through description leads to circularity—you need to already know it. When you reflect on your faith, do you rely on Allah's descriptions of Himself (through revelation) or do you seek to define Him on your own terms? Which approach leads to a deeper certainty?
?
Consider the objection: the same logic used to deny composition of existence would, if taken as a general proof, deny composition to all things (like a house). This challenges the proof itself. Are you willing to have your most confident logical arguments about God tested and critiqued? How does this affect your intellectual humility before the divine?

The Counter-Argument (Naqd) Against the Impossibility of Composite Existence
The author refutes the Imam's objection with a counter-argument and solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a counter-argument (naqd) to refute the claim that existence cannot be composite, and how the house example illustrates the fallacy.

Core Idea
If the argument against composite existence were sound, no composite quiddity would be possible.

The author begins by presenting the Imam's objection that existence cannot be composite, then immediately turns to a powerful counter-argument. By showing that the same reasoning would invalidate ordinary composites like a house, he exposes the flaw in the objection. This lesson walks through that exchange and the initial solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening objection
Read the Arabic passage from the start through the Imam's report. Notice how the objection is framed: parts must be either existential or non-existential, both leading to impossibilities.
The Imam's argument is dense; focus on the two alternatives.
2
Follow the counter-argument
The author replies with a counter-argument (naqd). He applies the same logic to a house: if the reasoning were valid, no house could exist. See how he draws the parallel.
The house example makes the abstract argument concrete.
3
Identify the solution
The author then gives the solution (hall): existence is a whole none of whose parts is itself existence, just as a house is a whole none of whose parts is a house. Understand what he means by 'whole' and 'parts'.
He uses the analogy of ten units: none of the units is ten.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: Why is this counter-argument effective? How does it avoid the original objection? The key is that parts can be things to which existence applies accidentally.
This prepares for the next lesson on intellectual parts.
