D) The relationship between a cause and its effect.

Key Takeaway
Existence is self-actualizing and does not need another existence, analogously to time's parts being inherently ordered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between 'self-necessary' and 'self-actualizing'. When you look at your own life, do you ever act as if you are self-necessary—independent of Allah? In what specific area do you need to remember that your existence is actualized by another, not by yourself?
?
The analogy of time says its parts are inherently ordered without needing another time. Think of a time you tried to fix a problem by seeking a second 'existence'—a secondary cause—when the primary cause was already sufficient. How can you train yourself to see the sufficiency of Allah's existence in your daily affairs?
?
You have just read that existence is not an accident added to things but is their actualization itself. Does your heart truly believe that your own existence is not an accident? What would change if you lived every moment knowing that your very being is a continuous actualization from Allah, not a static property?

Existence as Non-Existent: A Logical Clarification
Al-Taftazani argues that existence can be considered non-existent without contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Taftazani's alternative argument that existence can be said to be non-existent without contradiction, and the distinction between predication of existence and being a subject of existence.

Core Idea
Existence is not a possessor of existence, just as blackness is not black.

Al-Taftazani presents a second response to the objection: one may choose that existence is non-existent. This does not imply a contradiction because 'non-existent' here means that existence is not a subject of the property of existence. He clarifies that this is no more problematic than saying blackness is not black. This lesson explores this logical nuance and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on existence being non-existent
Read the Arabic and translation for the second option. Pay attention to the statement that 'existence is non-existent' and the justification.
2
Understand the logical distinction
Al-Taftazani explains that 'non-existent' here does not mean absolute non-existence but rather that existence is not a possessor (dhu) of existence. He compares it to blackness not being black.
This avoids the problem of describing existence with its own opposite.
3
Consider the objection about subsistence
Note the objection that if existence is non-existent, then it cannot subsist in a locus. Al-Taftazani responds that the subsistence in question is intellectual, not external.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saying 'existence is non-existent' is a direct contradiction because non-existent is the opposite of existent.
Correction: Al-Taftazani explains that 'non-existent' here means 'not a possessor of existence', not absolute non-existence, so no contradiction occurs.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Taftazani, what is the correct interpretation of 'existence is non-existent'?
A) Existence does not exist at all.
B) Existence is not a subject (possessor) of existence.
C) Existence is a privation.
D) Existence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Existence can be described as non-existent without contradiction, meaning it is not a subject of existence.

?

Where in your life are you treating non-material realities as less real?

How could a deeper awareness of the 'intellectual subsistence' of divine truths change the sincerity of your worship today?

Existence is Self-Subsistent
Taftazani proves existence is self-subsistent using a time analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's proof that existence is self-subsistent and why this does not lead to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Existence is self-realized; it doesn't need another existence to support it.

Taftazani begins his response to a second objection by asserting that existence exists inherently, not as an attached attribute. He argues that since everything is realized through existence, existence itself must be realized by itself, without need for another existence. This lesson walks through his central argument and the illuminating analogy with time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Understand the main claim
Identify Taftazani's position: He chooses that existence exists, and this does not lead to infinite regress.
The phrase 'لا يلزم التسلسل' is key.
3
Examine the time analogy
Consider the comparison with time. Just as parts of time are inherently prior and posterior without needing another time, existence is self-realized without needing another existence.
The analogy is used to illustrate self-subsistence.
4
Ask why this matters
Reflect on why establishing existence as self-subsistent is crucial for kalam: it provides a foundation for arguments about God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثاني: أنا نختار أن الوجود موجود، ولا يلزم التسلسل واللازم[؟] ويلزم لو كان الوجود أيضًا رائدًا عليه، وليس كذلك بل وجوده عينه أو إثمار النواع في غيره، والأدلة إنما قامت عليها وتحقيق ذلك أنه لما كان تحقق كل شيء بالوجود، فبالضرورة يكون تحققه بنفسه، من غير احتياج إلى وجود آخر يقوم به. كما أنه لما كان التقدم والتأخر فيما بين الأشياء بالزمان كائنًا فيما بين أجزائه بالذات من غير افتقار إلى زمان آخر ساعة بين له [؟] كالحمار. فإن قيل ليكون كل وجود واجبًا إذ لا معنى لها سوى الذي يكون متحققًا بنفسه؟ قلنا ممنوع فإن معنى وجود الواجب نفسه أنه لمقتضى ذاته من غير احتياج إلى فاعل، ومعنى تحقق الوجود بنفسه أنه إذا حصل للشيء إنه من ذاته كما في الواجب، أو من غيره كما في الممكنات لم يفتقر تحققه إلى وجود آخر يقوم به بخلاف الإنسان فإنه إنما يتحقق بعد تأثير الفاعل بوجود يقوم به. العقلان على أن في قولنا تحقق الأشياء بالوجود تسامحًا في العبارة، إذ الوجود نفس التحقق لا ماهية تحققها، والمعنى أن تحقق الأشياء يكون عند قيام الوجود بها.
Regarding the second [objection]: We choose that existence exists, and [this] does not entail an infinite regress and the necessary [consequence][?]. And it would entail if existence were also an attribute of it, but it is not so; rather its existence is identical to it or the [cause?] of its realization in others. And the proofs are established upon that. The verification of that is that since the realization of everything is by existence, necessarily its realization is by itself, without need for another existence to subsist in it. Just as since priority and posteriority among things in time are inherent among its parts by essence without needing another time... [unclear] like a donkey. If it is said: Then every existence would be necessary, since there is no meaning to it except that which is self-realized? We say: That is prevented. For the meaning of the existence of the Necessary is that it is by virtue of its essence without need for an agent, and the meaning of the self-realization of existence is that when something occurs from itself as in the Necessary, or from another as in contingents, its realization does not need another existence to subsist in it, unlike man, who is only realized after the agent's effect by an existence subsisting in him. The [intelligent][?] consider that our saying 'the realization of things by existence' is a concession in expression, for existence is itself realization, not a quiddity whose realization is [something else]; rather the meaning is that the realization of things occurs when existence subsists in them.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a response to a second objection. The author first states his position: he chooses that existence exists, and this does not lead to an infinite regress. He argues that existence is self-subsistent; everything is realized by existence, so existence itself must be self-realized. He draws an analogy with time: just as the parts of time are prior and posterior by themselves without needing another time, so too existence is realized by itself without another existence to support it. Then he anticipates an objection: if existence is self-realized, then every existence would be necessary (since necessary is defined as self-subsistent). He answers by distinguishing the meaning of 'existence of the Necessary' (which is by itself without agent) and 'self-realization of existence' (which means that when existence occurs in a subject, whether by itself or from another, it does not require a further existence to support it, unlike a human whose existence depends on an agent and a separate existence). Finally, he clarifies a verbal point: when we say 'realization of things by existence' we are speaking loosely, because existence is not a quiddity that has realization; rather existence is itself realization—things are realized when existence subsists in them.

Key Terms
الوجود
متحقق بنفسه
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if existence is self-subsistent, then it would be an infinite chain of existences.
Correction: Taftazani argues that existence does not need another existence to support it, stopping the regress.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the time analogy in this passage?
A) To show that time is self-subsistent like existence
B) To prove that existence is temporal
C) To indicate that existence has parts
D) To argue that time requires another time

Key Takeaway
Existence is self-realized; it does not rely on another existence, preventing infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is self-realized, what does that say about anything you look to for ultimate security besides Allah? How does this truth confront your reliance on created things?
?
You are not the source of your own existence. How should this realization change the way you approach your daily activities and achievements?
?
The time analogy shows that priority and posteriority are intrinsic to time. In the same way, your dependence on Allah is intrinsic to your being. When you feel self-sufficient, how can you remind yourself that only existence itself (ultimately Allah) is truly self-subsistent?

Distinguishing Necessary from Self-Subsistent
Taftazani refutes an objection by distinguishing necessity from self-subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why self-subsistent existence does not make everything necessary, and the linguistic clarification about existence and realization.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence does not imply necessity; it only means existence doesn't need a further support.

After arguing that existence is self-subsistent, Taftazani anticipates a powerful objection: if existence is self-realized, wouldn't every existence be necessary? In this lesson, we examine his nuanced answer that distinguishes between the existence of the Necessary (self-caused) and the self-sufficiency of existence as a concept. He also clarifies a common verbal imprecision.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the second part of the passage, from 'فإن قيل' to the end, with its translation.
Carefully note the distinction Taftazani makes.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says: If existence is self-subsistent, then every existence would be necessary (because necessary is defined as self-subsistent).
This is a serious challenge.
3
Grasp Taftazani's reply
Taftazani replies by differentiating the meaning of 'existence of the Necessary' (per se without agent) from 'self-realization of existence' (not needing a second existence to subsist in).
This preserves necessity for God alone.
4
Note the linguistic clarification
He adds that saying 'realization of things by existence' is a figure of speech. Existence is not a quiddity; it is itself realization.
This prevents misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a response to a second objection. The author first states his position: he chooses that existence exists, and this does not lead to an infinite regress. He argues that existence is self-subsistent; everything is realized by existence, so existence itself must be self-realized. Existence as Non-Existent
Taftazani introduces the alternative view that existence is non-existent and defends it from the objection of opposite description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's argument that existence can be considered non-existent without contradiction, using the analogy of blackness not being black.

Core Idea
Existence is not an existent; it is non-existent like blackness is not black.

Taftazani presents a bold alternative: rather than existence being a real attribute, it is non-existent. He argues that calling existence non-existent does not entail describing it by its opposite, because the opposite of existence is not 'non-existent' but 'not-existent'. This subtle distinction avoids self-contradiction, analogous to how blackness itself is not black.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative claim
Read Taftazani's statement that existence is non-existent. Notice how he frames it as a choice.
The phrase 'أو نختار' indicates he is offering an alternative to other views.
2
Analyze the opposite objection
Consider how the opponent might object that this makes existence its own opposite. Taftazani clarifies the meaning of 'opposite'.
He distinguishes between 'non-existent' (معدوم) and 'not-existent' (لا وجود).
3
Understand the blackness analogy
Reflect on how blackness is not black, yet we call things black. This shows the concept is not identical to its instances.
4
Grasp the mental subsistence
Realize that existence's inherence in quiddity is mental, not external, so it does not require real being.
This prevents the problem of something unreal inhering.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو نختار أن الوجود معدوم ولا يلزم اتصاف بنقيضه، بمعنى صدقه عليه، لأن نقيض الوجود هو العدم واللا وجود لا المعدوم، ولا اللاموجود، فغاية الأمر أنه يلزم أن الوجود ليس بذي وجود، كما أن السواد ليس بذي سواد، والأمر كذلك، ولا يلزم أيضًا أن يتحقق في المحل ما لا تحقق له في نفسه لما عرفت من أن قيام الموجود بالماهية ليس بحسب الخارج، كقيام البياض بالجسم، بل بحسب العقل فلا يلزم إلا تحققه في العقل. يجاب عن الأول بأنه منقوض بالأعراض القائمة بالمحال كسواد العمل. وقد النجم فإن قيامه إما بالجسم الأسود، فدور أو تسلسل والاجتماع لازم الأسود، فتناقض، وهو ضعيف لأن قيامه بجسم أسود به لا بسواد قبله ليلزم مجال، وطريانه على محل لا أسود يصير حال طريانه أسود من غير تناقض، ولا كذلك حال الوجود مع الماهية، لأن الخصم يدعي أن تقديم الماهية على العارض بالوجود ضروري، فلا يصح قيام الوجود بمحل موجود بهذا الوجود فلا محيص سوى المنع، والاستناد بأن ذلك إنما هو في العروض الخارجي، كمواد الجسم، وهذا ليس كذلك. بقولي الثانية بأن الوجود ليس بالموجود ولا المعدوم، وهنا [؟] الضعيف لما [؟] من نقي الواسطة [؟]
Or we choose that existence is non-existent, and it does not entail its being described by its opposite, in the sense of its being true of it, because the opposite of existence is non-existence and not-existent, not the non-existent nor the not-existent. So the upshot is that it entails that existence does not have existence, just as blackness does not have blackness, and the matter is so. Nor does it entail that what has no realization in itself becomes realized in a subject, because you know that the subsistence of an existent in a quiddity is not according to external [reality], like the subsistence of whiteness in a body, but according to the intellect; so it only entails its realization in the intellect. The first [objection] is answered by [the fact that] it is refuted by accidents subsisting in impossible subjects, like the blackness of the work. And further: the star, its subsistence is either in a black body, so that is a circle or infinite regress, and the conjunction of the non-black becomes necessary, so it is contradictory. But this is weak, because its subsistence in a black body, but not by a prior blackness, would require a [؟] and its occurring on a non-black subject makes it black at the time of occurrence without contradiction. The case of existence with quiddity is not so, because the opponent claims that the priority of quiddity to the attribute of existence is necessary, so existence cannot subsist in a subject that exists by this existence, so there is no escape except denial, and the appeal that this is only in external inherence, like the matter of body, and this is not the case. My second statement that existence is neither existent nor non-existent, and here the weak [argument؟] for the [؟] of the mean [؟]
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative response: the author chooses that existence is non-existent. He argues that calling existence 'non-existent' does not entail applying its opposite to it, because the opposite of existence is non-existence (al-`adam) and not-existent (al-la wujud), but existence itself is not a thing that can be described as existent or non-existent in that sense. The consequence is only that existence does not have existence, just as blackness is not black. Further, he addresses the objection that something unreal in itself cannot inhere in a subject; he explains that the inherence of existence in quiddity is not external like whiteness in a body, but mental, so it only needs mental realization. Then he responds to a counterexample about blackness inhering in a black body leading to regress or contradiction, showing that it is weak because the subject becomes black at the moment of inherence. Finally, he reiterates his second statement that existence is neither existent nor non-existent, defending against objections about the excluded middle.

Key Terms
معدوم
نقيض
السواد
القيام
عقلي
خارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that calling existence non-existent means existence is its own opposite.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that the opposite of existence is not 'non-existent' but 'not-existent', so there is no contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's alternative, why does calling existence 'non-existent' not entail describing it by its opposite?
A) Because the opposite of existence is not-existent, not non-existent
B) Because existence is not a thing
C) Because the opposite is absurd
D) Because existence is both existent and non-existent

Key Takeaway
Existence can be non-existent without contradiction because its opposite is 'not-existent', not 'non-existent', and its inherence is mental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you instinctively treat existence as a 'thing' in the same way you treat physical objects? How does that habit shape your conception of Allah's existence, and what must you correct in light of this lesson's distinction between mental and external reality?
?
The analogy 'blackness is not black' reveals that attributes are not self-applicable. Where in your understanding of Allah's attributes have you unconsciously assumed an attribute must apply to itself? How can you guard against that anthropomorphism?
?
Taftazani argues that something unreal (ma'dum) can still inhere mentally. This challenges your reliance on sensory evidence for truth. Are you willing to accept theological truths that have no external correlate? What is at stake if you refuse?

Existence Neither Existent Nor Non-Existent
Taftazani refutes an objection and states that existence is neither existent nor non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's final position that existence is neither existent nor non-existent, and how he refutes objections about the excluded middle.

Core Idea
Existence is a concept that is neither existent nor non-existent, avoiding the excluded middle fallacy.

Having argued that existence is non-existent, Taftazani now faces further objections. He refutes a counterexample involving blackness inhering in a black body, then states his final position: existence is neither existent nor non-existent. This resolves the apparent paradox and distinguishes existence from existent entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read how Taftazani responds to the blackness in a black body example. Note his refutation.
The objection tries to show a contradiction, but Taftazani shows it is weak.
2
Contrast with existence
See why the opponent's claim about priority of quiddity makes the existence case different.
The opponent's assumption leads to impossibility, so Taftazani denies it.
3
State the final position
Read the concluding statement that existence is neither existent nor non-existent.
This avoids the excluded middle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو نختار أن الوجود معدوم ولا يلزم اتصاف بنقيضه، بمعنى صدقه عليه، لأن نقيض الوجود هو العدم واللا وجود لا المعدوم، ولا اللاموجود، فغاية الأمر أنه يلزم أن الوجود ليس بذي وجود، كما أن السواد ليس بذي سواد، والأمر كذلك، ولا يلزم أيضًا أن يتحقق في المحل ما لا تحقق له في نفسه لما عرفت من أن قيام الموجود بالماهية ليس بحسب الخارج، كقيام البياض بالجسم، بل بحسب العقل فلا يلزم إلا تحققه في العقل. يجاب عن الأول بأنه منقوض بالأعراض القائمة بالمحال كسواد العمل. وقد النجم فإن قيامه إما بالجسم الأسود، فدور أو تسلسل والاجتماع لازم الأسود، فتناقض، وهو ضعيف لأن قيامه بجسم أسود به لا بسواد قبله ليلزم مجال، وطريانه على محل لا أسود يصير حال طريانه أسود من غير تناقض، ولا كذلك حال الوجود مع الماهية، لأن الخصم يدعي أن تقديم الماهية على العارض بالوجود ضروري، فلا يصح قيام الوجود بمحل موجود بهذا الوجود فلا محيص سوى المنع، والاستناد بأن ذلك إنما هو في العروض الخارجي، كمواد الجسم، وهذا ليس كذلك. بقولي الثانية بأن الوجود ليس بالموجود ولا المعدوم، وهنا [؟] الضعيف لما [؟] من نقي الواسطة [؟]
Or we choose that existence is non-existent, and it does not entail its being described by its opposite, in the sense of its being true of it, because the opposite of existence is non-existence and not-existent, not the non-existent nor the not-existent. So the upshot is that it entails that existence does not have existence, just as blackness does not have blackness, and the matter is so. Nor does it entail that what has no realization in itself becomes realized in a subject, because you know that the subsistence of an existent in a quiddity is not according to external [reality], like the subsistence of whiteness in a body, but according to the intellect; so it only entails its realization in the intellect. The first [objection] is answered by [the fact that] it is refuted by accidents subsisting in impossible subjects, like the blackness of the work. And further: the star, its subsistence is either in a black body, so that is a circle or infinite regress, and the conjunction of the non-black becomes necessary, so it is contradictory. But this is weak, because its subsistence in a black body, but not by a prior blackness, would require a [؟] and its occurring on a non-black subject makes it black at the time of occurrence without contradiction. The case of existence with quiddity is not so, because the opponent claims that the priority of quiddity to the attribute of existence is necessary, so existence cannot subsist in a subject that exists by this existence, so there is no escape except denial, and the appeal that this is only in external inherence, like the matter of body, and this is not the case. My second statement that existence is neither existent nor non-existent, and here the weak [argument؟] for the [؟] of the mean [؟]
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative response: the author chooses that existence is non-existent. He argues that calling existence 'non-existent' does not entail applying its opposite to it, because the opposite of existence is non-existence (al-`adam) and not-existent (al-la wujud), but existence itself is not a thing that can be described as existent or non-existent in that sense. The consequence is only that existence does not have existence, just as blackness is not black. Further, he addresses the objection that something unreal in itself cannot inhere in a subject; he explains that the inherence of existence in quiddity is not external like whiteness in a body, but mental, so it only needs mental realization. Then he responds to a counterexample about blackness inhering in a black body leading to regress or contradiction, showing that it is weak because the subject becomes black at the moment of inherence. Finally, he reiterates his second statement that existence is neither existent nor non-existent, defending against objections about the excluded middle.

Key Terms
السواد
القيام
العروض
خارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that everything must be either existent or non-existent (excluded middle).
Correction: Taftazani shows that existence as a concept is neither, avoiding the excluded middle.

Checkpoint
What is Taftazani's final position on the status of existence?
A) Existence is existent
B) Existence is non-existent
C) Existence is neither existent nor non-existent
D) Existence is both

Key Takeaway
Existence is neither existent nor non-existent; it is a concept that transcends the dichotomy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to prove God's existence by treating existence as an attribute that must belong to Him? How does this lesson's claim that existence is neither existent nor non-existent confront that approach? Will you reconsider how you articulate Allah's existence?
?
When you imagine the existence of Allah, do you subconsciously treat it as a 'thing' or property? How does realizing that existence transcends the categories of existent and non-existent change the way you relate to Him? Are you willing to let go of that mental image?
?
This lesson shows that the law of excluded middle does not apply to the concept of existence. What does that tell you about the limits of human logic when approaching divine realities? Will you humbly accept that some truths escape your rational categories and seek knowledge with that awareness?

Three Schools on Existence and Essence
Classification of three positions on existence as additional to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical positions on whether existence is additional to essence and why one of them is considered false.

Core Idea
Existence is either identical to essence, an accident, or a mystery—reason rejects the third.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational topic in Islamic theology and philosophy: the relationship between existence (wujud) and essence (mahiyya). Different schools have proposed distinct answers, and al-Sanusi evaluates them against the touchstone of rational intuition. Let's examine each position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose
The author aims to verify these doctrines in a way that does not contradict reason. This sets the criterion for evaluation.
Reason (badiha al-aql) is the ultimate judge.
2
Ibn Sina's view: existence equals essence
For Ibn Sina, the concept of existence is identical to the essence itself. For example, the existence of a human is just 'rational animal'. Existence terms vary with each existent.
This is known as the identity theory.
3
The mutakallimun's view: existence is an accident
The mutakallimun hold that existence is an accident (arad) that subsists in the essence, similar to other accidents inhering in their subjects.
Existence becomes a separate attribute.
4
The philosophers' view: split position
The philosophers agree with the mutakallimun for contingent things, but for the Necessary (God) they claim existence is a different meaning not grasped by intellects.
This dual position is criticized.
5
Author's judgment
The author dismisses the philosophers' view on God as 'clearly false' (zahir al-batlan), rejecting any claim that God's existence is beyond rational understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد تحقيق مذاهب الشيخ وسائر المتكلمين والحكماء على وجه لا يخالف بديهة العقل فإن الظاهر من مذهب الشيخ أن مفهوم وجود الإنسان هو الحيوان الناطق مثلاً ولفظ الوجود في العربية ولفظ (هستي) في الفارسية إلى غير ذلك معان لا تكاد تتناهى من الموجودات ومن مذهب المتكلمين أن الوجود عرض قائم بالماهية قيام سائر الأعراض بمحالها. ومن مذهب الحكماء أنه كذلك في الممكنات وفي الواجب معنى آخر غير مدرك للعقول، ذلك ظاهر البطلان
He intends to verify the doctrines of the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) and the rest of the mutakallimun and the philosophers in a way that does not contradict the primary intuition of reason. For what is apparent from the doctrine of the Shaykh is that the concept of the existence of a human is, for example, 'rational animal', and the word 'existence' in Arabic and the word 'hasti' in Persian—meanings almost limitless from existents. And from the doctrine of the mutakallimun is that existence is an accident subsisting in the essence, like the subsistence of other accidents in their subjects. And from the doctrine of the philosophers is that it is so in contingents, but in the Necessary it is another meaning not grasped by intellects; that is clearly false.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage classifies three schools of thought on whether existence is additional to essence. The author begins by stating his purpose: to verify the doctrines of the Shaykh (Ibn Sina), the mutakallimun, and the philosophers in a way that agrees with reason. He first explains the Shaykh's view: for him, the concept of existence is merely the concept of the thing itself—e.g., existence of a human is just 'rational animal', and existence has many meanings corresponding to each existent. The mutakallimun hold that existence is an accident that inheres in the essence. The philosophers (hukama') agree with the mutakallimun for contingent things, but for the Necessary (God), they claim existence is a different meaning beyond intellectual grasp. The author dismisses this last claim as clearly false.

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
عرض
بديهة العقل
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' view on the Necessary is not false but esoteric and acceptable.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the philosophers' claim about God's existence being beyond reason is 'clearly false' (zahir al-batlan), as it contradicts the principle that rational intuition is the standard.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which school holds that existence is an accident subsisting in the essence?

B) The mutakallimun
C) The philosophers (hukama')
D) The author of al-Saha'if

Key Takeaway
The three schools offer different answers on whether existence is part of essence or added, but the philosophers' special claim about God being beyond reason is rejected as clearly false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author insists that rational inquiry must not contradict primary intuition. Have you ever felt a tension between what your reason tells you and what you have been taught about God? How do you resolve that?
?
If existence is merely an accident added to essence, what does that imply about your own contingency? Does seeing yourself as a being that could have not existed deepen your gratitude to Allah? How would you live differently today if you truly felt that?

The Philosophers' Definition of the Necessary Being as Pure Existence
Defines pure existence and argues against composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of the Necessary Being as pure existence and their argument that He cannot have both essence and existence.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being is pure existence, not a composite of essence and existence.

The philosophers begin by defining the Necessary Being as existence itself, not something that has existence. This radical claim is the foundation of their entire theology. Let's explore their reasoning and why they think having an essence would lead to composition or need.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the philosophers describe the Necessary Being as existence by itself, without a maker or substrate.
The text uses 'pure existence' (al-wujūd al-baḥt) as a technical term.
2
Understand the definition
Identify what it means to be 'pure existence' – existence without an essence, not even mentally subsisting in an essence.
Compare with contingent existences, which are 'subjects for absolute existence'.
3
Follow the argument against having an essence
See why the philosophers reject both essence and existence: if the Necessary were the composite of both, composition would be impossible; if only one, then need arises.
The key point: each part would need the other.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that the philosophers believe the Necessary Being must be existence alone, without any essence, to be truly independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند الفلاسفة حقيقته وجود [؟] بذاته ذهناً وعيناً، من غير افتقار إلى فاعل يوجده، أو محل يقوم به، وهو مخالف لوجودات الممكنات بالحقيقة، وإن كان مشاركاً لها في كونه معروضاً للوجود المطلق، ويعبرون عنه بالوجود البحت، وبالوجود [؟] بمعنى أنه لا يقوم بماهية ولو في العقل، كما في وجود الممكنات. وإنما ذهبوا إلى ذلك لاعتقادهم أنه لو كان له ماهية ووجود، فإن كان الواجب هو المجموع لزم التركيب [؟]، وإن كان أحدهما [؟] لزم احتياجه، ضرورة احتياج الماهية في تحققها إلى الوجود، واحتياج الوجود لعروضه إلى الماهية ولو في العقل. وحين اعترض عليهم بأن الوجود الخاص أيضاً محتاج إلى الوجود المطلق، ضرورة امتناع تحقق الخاص بدون العام، أجابوا بأنه كون خاص [؟] متحقق بنفسه لا بالفاعل، قائم بذاته لا بالماهية، غني في التحقق عن الوجود المطلق وغيرها من العوارض والأسباب، مخالف لسائر الوجودات بالحقيقة وإن [؟].
And according to the philosophers, His reality is existence [?] by Himself mentally and externally, without needing a maker to bring Him into existence, or a substrate to subsist in, and it is different from the existences of contingent things in reality, although it shares with them in being a subject for absolute existence, and they call it pure existence, and existence [?] meaning that it does not subsist in an essence even mentally, as is the case with the existence of contingent things. They adopted this because they believed that if He had an essence and existence, then if the Necessary were the composite, composition would follow [?], and if one of them [were Him], then need would follow, because the essence needs existence for its realization, and existence needs an essence for its accidentally even mentally. And when it was objected against them that particular existence itself also needs absolute existence, since it is impossible for the particular to be realized without the universal, they answered that it is a particular [?] realized by itself not by an agent, subsisting by itself not by an essence, independent in realization from absolute existence and other accidents and causes, differing from all existences in reality although [?].
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' view that the Necessary Being (al-wājib) is pure existence (al-wujūd al-baḥt) without an essence (māhiyyah). They argue that if He had both an essence and an existence, it would lead to either composition (tarkīb) or dependence (iftiqār). If He were the composite of essence and existence, composition would be necessary, which is impossible for the Necessary. If only one of them were His reality, the other would be an attribute needing the essence, and the essence would need existence, leading to dependence. The passage also addresses an objection: even particular existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ) depends on absolute existence (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq). The philosophers respond that the particular existence of the Necessary is self-subsistent, independent of absolute existence and all other causes, and different from all contingent existences in reality.

Key Terms
الوجود البحت
الوجود المطلق
الماهية
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can't the Necessary Being have both an essence and an existence?
A) It would imply composition or need.
B) It would make Him observable.
C) It would contradict revelation.
D) It would make Him temporal.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define the Necessary Being as pure existence, free from any essence, to avoid composition and dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the philosophers define the Necessary Being as pure existence, free from any essence. Does this change the way you think about your own dependence on God? In your daily life, do you act as if God has needs or is composed of parts? What would it mean to truly believe He is pure existence, self-sufficient?
?
The philosophers argued that if God had an essence separate from His existence, it would lead to composition or need. Have you ever inadvertently thought of God as having a 'nature' that requires existence? How can you purify your concept of God from such anthropomorphic assumptions?
?
This definition of God as pure existence is a deep metaphysical concept. But does it affect your worship? When you pray, do you feel connected to a Being who is not like anything else, not even having a quiddity? How can you let this truth transform your humility and awe before Him?

Objection and Reply: The Independence of the Necessary Being's Existence
Presents the objection and the philosophers' reply about independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that even particular existence depends on absolute existence and how the philosophers reply that the Necessary Being's existence is self-subsistent and independent.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's existence is self-subsistent and independent, unlike all other existences.

The philosophers' view that the Necessary is pure existence without essence invites an obvious objection: even particular existence depends on universal absolute existence. How can the Necessary be independent? The philosophers respond with a nuanced distinction. Let's examine their reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector argues that even the particular existence of the Necessary depends on absolute existence, because the particular cannot exist without the universal.
Note that 'particular existence' here means the specific existence of the Necessary.
2
Read the philosophers' reply
The philosophers respond that the Necessary's existence is a 'particular' that is realized by itself, not by an agent, subsisting by itself, not by an essence.
They claim it is independent of absolute existence and all other causes.
3
Understand the distinction
The key is that the Necessary's existence is not dependent on anything else; it is a particular that is self-sufficient, unlike contingent particulars that require a universal and a cause.
This maintains the transcendence of the Necessary.
4
Reflect on the implications
This reply reinforces the idea that the Necessary Being is utterly unique and not subject to the metaphysical rules of contingent beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Essence and Existence in the Necessary Being: Theologians vs. Philosophers
The author states the mutakallimun and philosophers' views on the Necessary Being's existence and gives the philosophers' argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two contrasting views on whether existence is additional to essence in the Necessary Being, and the philosophers' reasoning that His reality must be existence itself.

Core Idea
For God, existence is not added to essence; philosophers say He is pure existence.

This lesson introduces a key debate in Islamic theology: is God's existence something over and above His essence, or is His very reality existence itself? Theologians and philosophers give different answers, with profound implications for divine simplicity. The passage you are about to read presents both positions and the philosophers' argument for their view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first the theologians' view, then the philosophers' view, followed by their reasoning.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two views
Find where the author describes the mutakallimun's view (theologians) and the falasifa's view (philosophers). Note the key terms: haqiqa (reality), wujud (existence), mahiyya (essence).
The theologians say God's reality necessitates existence; philosophers say reality is existence.
3
Analyze the philosophers' argument
Focus on the last sentence. The philosophers argue that if God had both essence and existence, it would lead either to composition (tarkib) or need (ihtiyaj). Understand each horn of the dilemma.
This argument is crucial for their conclusion.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why this matters: if God's essence is existence itself, then He is absolutely simple and independent. If there is a conceptual distinction, He could be seen as composed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما هو في الممكن، وأما في الواجب فعند المتكلمين له حقيقية غير مدركة للعقول مقتضية بذاتها لوجودها الخاص المغاير بحسب المفهوم دون الهوية، كما في الممكنات، وعند الفلاسفة حقيقته وجود بذاته ذهنا وعينا، من غير افتقار إلى فاعل يوجده، أو محل يقوم به. وإنما ذهبوا إلى ذلك لاعتقادهم أنه لو كان له ماهية ووجود فإن كان الواجب هو المجموع لزم التركيب، وإن كان أحدهما لزم احتياجه، ضرورة احتياج الماهية في تحققها إلى الوجود، واحتياج الوجود لعروضه إلى الماهية.
That [dispute] is only in the possible. As for the Necessary, according to the theologians (mutakallimun) He has a reality not perceived by intellects, necessitating by itself its own particular existence, differing conceptually but not in identity, as in contingent beings. According to the philosophers (falasifa), His reality is existence itself, mentally and externally, without need of an agent to bring Him into existence or a substrate to inhere in. They only held this because they believed that if He had an essence (mahiyya) and an existence (wujud), then if the Necessary were the composite, composition would follow, and if [He were] one of them, need would follow—necessarily because the essence needs existence for its actualization, and existence, due to its accidentally, needs the essence [for its instantiation].
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents two views on the relationship between essence and existence in the Necessary Being. The author first states that the dispute about existence being additional to essence—where the concept is distinct—is only about possible beings. For the Necessary, the theologians hold that He has an unknowable reality that necessitates His particular existence, which is distinct in concept but identical in identity. The philosophers, by contrast, hold that His very reality is existence, both mentally and externally, independent of any agent or substrate. The passage then gives the philosophers' reasoning: if the Necessary had both an essence and an existence, then either He would be the composite of the two—resulting in composition—or He would be one of them—implying need, because essence needs existence to be actual, and existence needs an essence to inhere in. Thus, to avoid composition and need, His essence must be existence itself.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
واجب (wajib)
ممكن (mumkin)
تركيب (tarkib)
احتياج (ihtiyaj)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute over essence and existence in God is just a semantic debate about words.
Correction: It is a real metaphysical dispute about whether God's essence and existence are identical or distinct, with implications for divine simplicity and independence.

Checkpoint
What is the view of the philosophers (falasifa) regarding the essence of the Necessary Being?
A) It is unknown to the intellect
B) It is existence itself
C) It is a composite of essence and existence
D) It is contingent like possible beings

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God's essence must be existence itself to avoid composition and need, while theologians maintain a conceptual distinction without real separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is a gift that could be taken away at any moment. God's existence is His very essence. When you feel self-sufficient, does your heart forget that you are nothing without Him? Will you today pause and say, 'My existence is from You alone'?
?
The philosophers argued that if God had separate essence and existence, He would need something else. Do you ever treat God as if He needs your worship or your obedience? How can you shift your intention from 'giving God something' to 'receiving existence from Him every moment'?
?
If God is pure existence, then everything that exists does so only by Him. What is one area of your life where you act as if you are the source of your own success? Will you consciously attribute that to Him today and thank Him for the very act of existing?

Objection: Specific Existence Needs Absolute Existence and Reply
The author presents an objection that specific existence needs absolute existence and gives the reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why specific existence is not dependent on absolute existence according to the philosophers, and the distinction between essential and accidental concomitance.

Core Idea
Particular existence is self-subsistent and absolute existence is only an accidental concomitant.

This lesson presents a key objection against the independence of the Necessary Being's specific existence. The objector argues that even particular existence requires absolute existence. The philosophers respond by clarifying the relationship: absolute existence is merely an accidental, external concomitant, not a constituent. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the self-sufficiency of the divine nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the structure: objection then answer.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objector's claim
Find the reason given for the objection: 'impossibility of the particular actualizing without the universal.' Understand the logic.
3
Examine the reply's key points
The answer states that specific existence is 'actualized by itself', 'self-subsistent', and 'independent of absolute existence'. List these characteristics.
Notice the contrasts: bi-nafsihi vs bi-l-fa'il, qa'im bi-dhatihi vs bi-l-mahiyya.
4
Understand the status of absolute existence
The passage says absolute existence occurs as an 'accidental, external, non-constitutive concomitant'. This means it is not a part of specific existence.
The term 'lazim khariji' is key.
5
Draw the conclusion
Because of these points, the specific existence does not become composite or needy. Reflect on why this matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحين اعترض عليهم بأن الوجود الخاص أيضا محتاج إلى الوجود المطلق، ضرورة امتناع تحقق الخاص بدون العام، أجابوا بأنه كون خاص متحقق بنفسه لا بالفاعل، قائم بذاته لا بالماهية، غني في التحقق عن الوجود المطلق وغيرها من العوارض والأسباب، مخالف لسائر الوجودات بالحقيقية وإن كان مشاركاً لها في وقوع الوجود المطلق عليها وقوع لازم خارجي غير مقوم، وهذا لا يوجب التركيب ولا الافتقار.
When it was objected against them that the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas) is also in need of absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq), given the impossibility of the particular actualizing without the universal, they answered that it is a particular individuation (kawn khas) actualized by itself, not by an agent, self-subsistent, not by an essence, independent in actualization of absolute existence and of other accidents and causes, different from all existences in reality, even though it shares with them the occurrence of absolute existence upon it as an accidental, external, non-constitutive concomitant, and this does not necessitate composition or dependence.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that even the particular existence (the specific existence of the Necessary, for example) must be in need of absolute existence, because a particular cannot exist without the universal. The philosophers answer that particular existence is a self-subsistent individuation, actual by itself, not dependent on an agent or essence, and independent of absolute existence. While it is true that absolute existence occurs upon it as an accidental concomitant, this does not make it a constituent or cause dependence. Thus, the specific existence of the Necessary is not needy—it is its own reality.

وجود مطلق (wujud mutlaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Absolute existence is a constituent part of particular existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that absolute existence is only an accidental, external concomitant (lazim khariji), not a constitutive element (muqawwim).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Specific existence is not dependent on absolute existence because it is self-subsistent and absolute existence is merely an accidental concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Self-Subsistent Reality Must Be Existence Itself
Objection and answer on the identity of existence and essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the self-subsistent, independent reality of the Necessary Being cannot be anything other than existence itself.

Core Idea
Anything that is self-subsistent must be existence itself.

We now encounter a key objection: could the unique reality that is self-subsistent and independent be something other than existence—perhaps a special kind of quiddity? The answer reveals a fundamental principle of Islamic metaphysics: whatever is not existence (i.e., any essence) requires existence to become actual. A truly self-actualized reality cannot be anything but existence. Let us examine this concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection is raised, then a reply is given.
The objection asks 'why not something other than existence?' The reply is a direct refutation.
2
Reflect on the principle
Consider the key principle: anything that is not existence (i.e., a quiddity) needs existence to become actual. How does this show that the self-subsistent reality must be existence itself?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحين اعترض بأنه لم لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الحقيقة المخالفة لسائر الحقائق المتحققة بنفسها الغنية عما سواها أمراً غير الوجود؟ أجابوا بأن المتحقق بنفسه الغني عما سواه لا يجوز أن يكون غير الوجود لأن احتياج غير الموجود في التحقق إلى الوجود ضروري.
When it was objected: 'Why may it not be that that reality, which is different from all other realities, actualized by itself, independent of everything else, is something other than existence?' They answered: 'That which is actualized by itself, independent of everything else, cannot be other than existence, because the need of that which is not existence for existence [to become actual] is necessary.'
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The objector questions why the self-subsistent, independent reality of the Necessary could not be something other than existence—perhaps a special type of quiddity. The philosophers reply succinctly: anything that is actualized by itself and independent of anything else must be existence itself, because anything other than existence (i.e., any essence or quiddity) requires existence to become actual. Since this reality is already self-actualized, it cannot be non-existence; it must be existence.

Key Terms
متحقق بنفسه (mutahaqqiq bi-nafsihi)
غني عما سواه (ghani 'amma siwah)
غير الوجود (ghayr al-wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a self-subsistent reality could be a special essence or quiddity that is not existence.
Correction: The philosophers argue that any non-existence (i.e., any essence apart from existence) necessarily depends on existence to become actual, so a self-subsistent, independent reality cannot be non-existence; it must be existence itself.

Checkpoint

C) Because non-existence needs existence to become actual
D) Because existence is the only real thing

Key Takeaway
A self-subsistent, independent reality cannot be non-existence because non-existence requires existence to become actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If Allah is existence itself, then turning away from Him is a kind of non-being. What are you clinging to that makes you feel real apart from Him, and what step will you take to let go of that illusion this week?

Objection: Existence Is One Concept Multiplied Only by Addition
Objection and reply: existence as accidental concomitant vs. single concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that existence is a single concept multiplied by addition, and the answer that existence is an accidental concomitant upon intrinsically different realities.

Core Idea
Existence is not a genus but an accidental commonality shared by different realities.

This passage tackles a key objection to the doctrine of the unicity of existence. The objector claims existence is one concept that only multiplies when added to essences. The reply denies this and uses analogies to show that commonality is accidental, not essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the claim: existence is a single concept that becomes multiple only by addition to essences, like whiteness of snow and ivory.
Notice the use of 'restricted' and 'absolute'.
2
Understand the denial
The philosophers deny that existences are merely shares of a single concept. They assert existences are multiple realities in themselves.
Identify the two rejected alternatives: being similar by addition or being species of a genus.
3
Examine the analogies
Study the analogies: light of sun and lamp, whiteness of snow and ivory, quantity and quality sharing accidentally, substance and accident sharing contingency.
Each analogy shows commonality is accidental, not essential.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Grasp the key point: existence is an accidental concomitant, not a genus or a single nature, thus compatible with the real multiplicity of existences.
This resolves the objector's concern without compromising divine simplicity or creaturely diversity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحين اعترض بأن الوجود مفهوم واحد لا يتكثر ولا يصير حصة حصة إلا بالإضافة إلى الماهيات كبياض هذا الثلج وذاك، إذ لا معنى للمقيد دون المطلق مع قيد الإضافة. أجابوا بمنع ذلك بل الوجودات حصص مختلفة وحقائق متكثرة بأنفسها، لا بمجرد عارض الإضافة لتكون متماثلة متفقة الحقيقة ولا بالفصول ليكون الوجود المطلق جنساً لها، بل هو عارض لازم لها كنور الشمس ونور السراج، فإنهما مختلفان بالحقيقة واللوازم مشتركان في عارض النور وكذا بياض الثلج والعاج، بل كالكم والكيف المشتركين في العرضية بل الجوهر والعرض المشتركان في الإمكان.
When it was objected that existence is a single concept that is not multiplied and does not become particular shares except by addition to essences, like the whiteness of this snow and that, for there is no meaning for the restricted without the absolute together with the restriction of addition. They answered by denying that. Rather, existences are different shares and multiple realities in themselves, not merely by the accident of addition such that they would be similar and agreeing in reality, nor by differentiae such that absolute existence would be a genus for them. Rather, it is an accidental concomitant for them, like the light of the sun and the light of a lamp—they are different in reality and concomitants, yet share in the accident of light—and likewise the whiteness of snow and ivory. Rather, like quantity and quality sharing in accidentally, and like substance and accident sharing in contingency.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents the final objection and its detailed answer. The objector argues that existence is a single concept that only becomes multiple 'shares' when added to different essences, just as whiteness is one concept but becomes 'whiteness of snow' and 'whiteness of ivory' by addition. The philosophers deny this. They assert that existences are intrinsically multiple and different realities, not merely different by the accidental relation of addition. If they were similar in reality, absolute existence would be a genus and they would be species, but they are not. Instead, absolute existence is an accidental concomitant (عارض لازم) that occurs upon them, like light occurring upon the substances of the sun and a lamp—two different realities that share the accident of light. Similarly, whiteness in snow and ivory are different realities sharing the accident of whiteness. Further analogies: quantity and quality share in being accidents, and substance and accident share in contingency. The point is that the commonality is accidental, not essential.

Key Terms
حصص مختلفة (hisas mukhtalifa)
عارض لازم (arid lazim)
نور الشمس (nur al-shams)
نور السراج (nur al-siraj)
الكم والكيف (al-kamm wa al-kayf)
الجوهر والعرض (al-jawhar wa al-ard)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is a genus that applies to all existents in the same essential way, making them species of a single category.
Correction: Existence is not a genus; it is an accidental concomitant that applies to intrinsically different realities.

Checkpoint
How do the philosophers explain the fact that existences share the concept of existence?
A) Existence is a genus and existences are its species
B) Existence is an accidental concomitant that occurs on different realities
C) Existence is an illusion and only particular essences are real
D) Existence is a mental construct with no external reality

Key Takeaway
Existence is an accidental concomitant, not a genus, that applies to fundamentally different realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of things as 'existing' in the same way—your phone, your friend, your own soul. After reading this lesson, do you still believe they all share existence essentially, or only accidentally? How does that change your reliance on temporary things versus the Eternal?
?
The philosophers say existences are different in themselves. If true, then every thing's existence is unique. Have you been treating the existence of your own life as just another instance of a generic 'being'? What would it mean for you to live as though your existence is a distinct reality that cannot be compared to others?
?
When you look at the diversity in creation—the sun's light and the lamp's light, snow and ivory—does this lesson help you see the Creator's wisdom in making every existent unique? Are you willing to stop lumping all beings together and instead appreciate each thing's distinct existence as a sign of God's creative power?

The Self-Subsisting Reality Must Be Existence
Objection and answer about reality vs. existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the self-subsisting reality of the Necessary Being cannot be anything other than existence, based on the necessary need of non-existence for existence.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence excludes non-existence because non-existence depends on existence.

In this brief objection-and-answer, the author addresses a critical question: could the unique, self-subsisting reality of the Necessary Being be something other than existence itself? The answer draws on a simple but profound logical point about dependence. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage: the objector suggests that the self-subsisting reality might be something other than existence.
Notice the objector does not deny self-subsistence, only that it must be existence.
2
Read the answer
Read the response: it asserts that self-subsisting being cannot be non-existence, because non-existence needs existence to become actual.
The key term is 'احتياج' (need) – non-existence is inherently dependent.
3
Identify the logical link
Understand why the answer refutes the objection: if something is self-sufficient (independent), it cannot be something that requires another for its actuality.
Existence is that which makes things actual; non-existence is its opposite.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this means for the nature of the Necessary Being: it is identical to existence, not a possessor of existence.
This is a key point in Islamic metaphysics.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp why the answer is logically necessary.

Source Text

وحين اعترض بأنه لم لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الحقيقة المخالفة لسائر الحقائق المتحققة بنفسها الغنية عما سواها أمراً غير الوجود . . ؟ أجابوا : بأن المتحقق بنفسه الغني عما سواه لا يجوز أن يكون غير الوجود لأن احتياج غير الموجود في التحقق إلى الوجود ضروري.

This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector suggests that the reality of the Necessary Being—which is self-subsisting and independent—might be something other than existence. The answer refutes this by stating that anything self-subsisting and independent cannot be non-existence (ma'dum), because non-existence inherently needs existence (wujud) to become actualized (tahaqquq). Thus, the self-subsisting reality must be existence itself.

A self-subsisting, independent reality cannot be non-existence because non-existence depends on existence for actualization; therefore, the Necessary Being must be existence itself.

Are Existences Distinct Realities? An Objection and Answer
Objection and answer arguing that existences are distinct realities, not mere conceptual portions added to essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection that existence is a single concept multiplied by essences and the refutation that existences are distinct realities in themselves, along with the analogies used to illustrate this point.

Core Idea
Existences are distinct realities, not mere conceptual additions to essences.

This lesson presents an important objection in Islamic theology: if existence is a single concept, how can multiple existences be distinguished? The objector argues that existence becomes multiple only by being added to different essences, like whiteness in snow and ivory. The answer refutes this, insisting that existences are inherently distinct realities, not merely conceptual portions. Through analogies like the light of the sun versus the light of a lamp, the author demonstrates that sharing a common attribute does not imply identity of essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objection that existence is a single concept that becomes multiple only by addition to essences. Note the example of whiteness in snow and that (other object).
The objector is relying on the idea that a universal becomes particularized only through its subject.
2
Identify the core of the answer
The answer denies that existences are merely conceptual portions. Instead, they are 'different portions and multiple realities in themselves' (ḥiṣaṣ mukhtalifa wa ḥaqāʾiq mutakaththira bi-anfusihā). Understand the key claim: existences are distinct by themselves, not by added accidents or differentiae.
This is a strong realist position on the multiplicity of existence.
3
Examine the analogies
The author uses several analogies: sunlight vs. lamplight (different in reality but both are light), whiteness of snow vs. ivory, quantity vs. quality, substance vs. accident. For each, note how they share a common attribute but differ in essence.
The analogies serve to show that a shared accidental predicate does not imply identity of the subjects.
4
Consider the qualification at the end
The passage ends with a nuanced statement: the view of real difference holds for necessary vs. contingent existence, is probable for substance vs. accident and stable vs. non-stable, but not for man vs. horse or Zayd vs. 'Amr. Reflect on why this qualification matters.
This shows the author's careful approach to different levels of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحين اعترض بأن الوجود مفهوم واحد لا يتكثر ولا يصير حصة حصة إلا بالإضافة إلى الماهيات كبياض هذا الثلج وذاك ، إذ لا معنى للمقيد دون المطلق مع قيد الإضافة . أجابوا بمنع ذلك بل الوجودات حصص مختلفة وحقائق متكثرة بأنفسها، لا بمجرد عارض الإضافة لتكون متماثلة متفقة الحقيقة ولا بالفصول ليكون الوجود المطلق جنساً لها، بل هو عارض لازم لها كنور الشمس ونور السراج، فإنهما مختلفان بالحقيقة واللوازم مشتركان في عارض النور وكذا بياض الثلج والعاج، بل كالكم والكيف المشتركين في العرضية بل الجوهر والعرض المشتركان في الإمكان ،والوجود إلا أنه لما لم يكن لكل وجود اسم خاص كما في أقسام الممكن وأقسام العرض وغير ذلك. توهم أن تكثر الوجودات وكونها حصة حصة إنما هو بمجرد الإضافة إلى الماهيات المعروضة لها كبياض هذا الثلج وذاك ونور هذا السراج وذاك، وليس كذلك، والإنصاف أن ما ذكروا من الاختلاف بالحقيقة حق في وجود الواجب والممكن ومحتمل في مثل وجود الجوهر والعرض ومثل وجود القار وغير القار، وأما في مثل وجود الإنسان والفرس ووجود زيد وعمرو فلا . [حاصل ما ذكروا التزام أن الوجود حقائق متباينة في أنفسها لا يتوقف تباينها وتحققها على الماهيات حتى يلزم افتقار الوجود إلى الماهية وأن ما يتوهم من الاتحاد في الحقيقة ليس كذلك، وسببه الأسامي وسند ذلك سائر حقائق المشكل .]
And when it was objected that existence is a single concept that does not become multiple nor become portions except by addition to essences, like the whiteness of this snow and that, since there is no meaning for the qualified without the absolute with the restriction of addition. They answered by denying that; rather existences are different portions and multiple realities in themselves, not merely by an accidental addition so that they would be similar agreeing in reality, nor by differentiae so that absolute existence would be a genus for them; rather it is an inseparable accident for them, like the light of the sun and the light of the lamp, for they are different in reality and concomitants while sharing in the accident of light, and likewise the whiteness of snow and ivory; nay like quantity and quality sharing in being accidents, nay like substance and accident sharing in contingency and existence — except that since every existence does not have a special name, as in the divisions of the contingent and the divisions of accident and otherwise, it was imagined that the multiplicity of existences and their being portions is only by addition to the essences that are substrates for them, like the whiteness of this snow and that and the light of this lamp and that, but it is not so. And fairness is that what they mentioned of difference in reality is true for the existence of the Necessary and the contingent, and probable for the existence of substance and accident and the existence of the stable and non-stable; but as for the existence of man and horse and the existence of Zayd and 'Amr, no.  [The summary of what they mentioned is the commitment that existences are distinct realities in themselves; their distinctness and actualization do not depend on essences such that existence would need essences, and that what is imagined of unity in reality is not so, and its cause is names, and the basis of that is other problematic realities.]
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection-and-answer. The objector argues that existence (wujud) is a single concept that becomes multiple only when added to different essences (mahiyyat), like whiteness applied to different objects. The answer refutes this by asserting that existences are different portions (hisas) and multiple realities (haqa'iq mutakaththira) in themselves, not merely by an accidental relation. Existence is an inseparable accident (arid lazim) for its subjects, analogous to light of sun and lamp: they differ in reality and concomitants but share the accident of light. Similarly, quantity and quality share being accidents, and substance and accident share contingency and existence. The confusion arises because many existences lack distinct names. The passage then qualifies that the view of real difference holds for necessary and contingent existence, is plausible for substance/accident and stable/unstable, but not for cases like man/horse or Zayd/'Amr (where existence is accidental?). The footnote summarizes that existences are distinct in themselves and do not depend on essences.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is merely a conceptual addition to essence, so all existences share the same reality of existence.
Correction: Existences are distinct realities in themselves; they are not made multiple simply by adding to essences. They differ in their intrinsic realities while sharing the attribute of existence.

Resolving the Objection of Two Existences
Objection and answer about absolute existence as accident leading to two existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that absolute existence as accident would entail two existences in the Necessary Being is resolved through the distinction between mental and external reality.

Core Idea
The objection of two existences is resolved by recognizing the mental nature of universal concepts.

This passage presents a crucial objection in the debate over whether existence is an accident. The objector argues that if absolute existence is an accident, then each reality would have a portion of existence, leading to absurd consequences. The answer clarifies the real point of contention and shows that the supposed two existences are a product of mental analysis, not external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the argument: if absolute existence is an accident, each entity has a portion of the concept of existence, leading to two existences for the Necessary and absurdities like two whitenesses in snow.
Notice the reductio ad absurdum.
2
Analyze the answer
The answer distinguishes the issue: the added portion of the concept is not disputed; rather, the dispute is whether the Necessary has an essence upon which existence is added. After tashkik, the difference is only mental.
Focus on the phrase 'but according to reason, not externally'.
3
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this resolves the objection without denying conceptual distinctions. The universal concept's portions are mental forms with no external reality.
This preserves the identity of the Necessary's essence and existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فإن قلت لو كان المطلق عارضاً لها لكان في كل منها حصة من مفهوم الكون كما هو شأن الأعراض العامة فتكون الحصة من مفهوم الكون زائداً على ما هو حقيقة الواجب كما في الممكنات، ويلزم فيه وجودان معروض وعارض. وفي الممكن وجودان وماهية ، وعلى هذا في الثلج بياضان ، وهذا مما يكذبه العقل والحس . قلت لا نزاع لهم في زيادة الحصة من مفهوم الكون على الوجود الخاص الذي هو حقيقة الواجب، إذ لا فرق بين مفهوم الكون والحصص منه إلا بمجرد اعتبار الإضافة، وإنما نزاعهم في أن تكون له ماهية يزيد عليها الوجود الخاص في التعقل وثبوته وبعد القول بالتشكيك فمغايرة الوجودات المخصص من مفهوم الكون ضروري لكن بحسب العقل دون الخارج، لما تقرر من اتحاد الموضوع والمحمول بحسب الذات، على أن الحصص من مفهوم العام صور عقلية لا تحقق لها في الأعيان، فلا يلزم للممكن وجودان ولا في الأبيض بياضان .
He said: For if you say: if the absolute were an accident to them, then in each of them there would be a portion from the concept of existence, as is the case with universal accidents, so the portion from the concept of existence would be additional to what is the reality of the Necessary, as in contingent things, and two existences would follow in Him: a substrate and an accident. And in the contingent, two existences and an essence; and on this, in snow there would be two whitenesses, and this is refuted by reason and sense. I say: they do not dispute the increase of the portion from the concept of existence over the particular existence which is the reality of the Necessary, since there is no difference between the concept of existence and the portions from it except by mere consideration of addition. Rather their dispute is whether He has an essence upon which the particular existence is additional in intellection and subsistence. And after the doctrine of tashkik, the difference of the particular existences from the concept of existence is necessary, but according to reason, not externally, because it is established that subject and predicate are identical in essence. Moreover, the portions from the universal concept are mental forms with no actuality in external entities, so for the contingent two existences are not necessary, nor for the white two whitenesses.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (fa-in qulta... qultu). The objector argues that if absolute existence (al-mutlaq) is an accident (arad) to the realities, then each reality would have a portion (hissa) from the concept of existence (mafhum al-kawn), making that portion additional to the reality itself. For the Necessary Being, this would imply two existences: the substrate (the particular existence) and the accident (the universal concept). Similarly, for a contingent, there would be two existences and an essence, and for snow two whitenesses, which is absurd. The answer (qultu) clarifies that the disputants do not deny that the portion of the concept of existence is additional to the particular existence that is the reality of the Necessary; rather, their dispute is whether the Necessary has an essence (mahiyya) upon which the particular existence is additional in intellection and subsistence. After accepting the doctrine of tashkik (gradation), the distinction between particular existences and the concept of existence is necessary only in reason, not externally, because subject and predicate are identical in essence. The portions of the universal concept are mental forms with no external reality, so no two existences follow for contingent nor two whitenesses for white things.

Correction: The answer shows that the supposed two existences are only a mental distinction; externally, the Necessary Being's existence is one. The portions of the universal concept are mental forms with no extramental reality.

Key Takeaway
The objection that absolute existence as accident would imply two existences is resolved by distinguishing between mental concepts and external reality; the portions of existence are merely mental forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you reflect on the concept of existence, do you realize that your mental picture of 'existence' is not the same as the actual existence of the Necessary Being? Write a commitment to remind yourself that your thoughts about Allah are mere mental forms, not the reality.
?
The source says that the portions of the universal concept are 'mental forms with no actuality in external entities.' Does this challenge your assumption that your understanding of tawhid is complete? If your concepts are only mental and not the external reality, how humble should you be in claiming to know Allah? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge the limitation of your intellect before Allah?
?
The objection about two whitenesses in snow is dismissed as absurd. Yet in your daily life, do you ever act as if there are two sources of existence—Allah and your own independent being? Is there an area where you still feel you have 'your own existence' apart from Allah's constant creation? Expose one area of independence you harbor and repent from it.
