The Fourth Derivative Issue: Certainty of the Creator Despite Doubt
Al-Taftazani presents the fourth tafri', its objection, answer, and concluding remark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth derivative issue regarding the permissibility of being certain about the Creator's attributes while doubting His existence, along with the objection and its two-part answer.

Core Idea
Certainty about God's attributes can precede proof of His existence.

Al-Taftazani presents a subtle theological scenario: can we be certain that the world has a Creator with attributes while still doubting His existence? The objector argues this leads to absurd consequences. In this lesson, we will trace the argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the entire argument.
Pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the claim
Note the main position: it is permissible to be certain of the Creator's attributes while doubting His existence until proof.
3
Understand the objection
The objector claims this would allow doubting the existence of bodies known by their attributes, leading to ignorance.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Study the answer
The author gives two responses: first, after conceiving the essence with attributes, doubt is permissible; second, conditional knowledge still allows doubt about actual existence.
The second answer does not depend on the non-existent having attributes.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Note the concluding remark that this issue only applies for those who hold that the non-existent has attributes.
This clarifies the scope of the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أنه يجوز القطع بأن للعالم صانعاً متصفاً بالعلم، والقدرة والحياة مع الشك في وجوده، حتى يقوم عليه البرهان وذلك لأنهم جوزوا اتصاف المعدوم الثابت بالصفات الثبوتية، واعترض بأن هذا يستلزم جواز الشك في وجود الأجسام بعد العلم باتصافها بالمتحركية والساكنية لجواز أن تتصف بذلك في العدم، فيحتاج وجودها إلى دلالة منفصلة، وذلك جهالة عظيمة . والجواب؛ بأنا بعد ما نتصور ذاتاً متصفة بتلك الصفات ونصدق بأن صانع العالم يجب أن يكون كذلك، يجوز أن نشك في أن للعالم صانعاً كذلك. أو بأنا بعد العلم بأن كل ما لو وجد كان صانع العالم فهو بحيث لو وجد كان متصفاً بتلك الصفات، يجوز أن نشك في أنه موجود في الخارج ليس بشيء لأنه لا يتفرع حينئذ على كون المعدوم شيئاً وثابتاً في الخارج، بل يصح على قول النافين أيضاً، ألا يرى أنا نستدل على وجود الواجب، ومعناه أن الذات المتصفة بوجوب الوجود يفتقر التصديق بوجودها إلى الدليل، ونقطع بأن شريك الباري ممتنع ومعناه أن الذات المتصفة بالوجود، وسائر صفات الكمال المغايرة للباري تعالى وتقدس تمتنع أن توجد في الخارج. واعلم أنهم وإن جعلوا هذا التفريع متفقاً عليه إلا أنه إنما رأي القائلين بأن للمعدوم صفة.
And [the fourth derivative issue] is that it is permissible to be certain that the world has a Maker qualified by knowledge, power, and life while doubting His existence, until proof is established. That is because they permitted the established non-existent to be qualified by positive attributes. And it was objected that this entails the permissibility of doubting the existence of bodies after knowing that they are qualified by motility and stasis, because it is possible that they are qualified by that in non-existence, so their existence would need a separate indication, and that is great ignorance. And the answer: [first] after we conceive an essence qualified by those attributes and affirm that the Maker of the world must be such, it is permissible for us to doubt that there is a Maker of the world like that. Or [second] after we know that everything that, if it existed, would be the Maker of the world, is such that if it existed it would be qualified by those attributes, it is permissible for us to doubt that it exists in the external world. This is not something because it does not then follow from the [principle] that the non-existent is a thing and established in the external world; rather it is also valid according to the deniers. Do you not see that we infer the existence of the Necessary, meaning that the essence qualified by necessity of existence, belief in its existence requires a proof; and we are certain that the partner of the Creator is impossible, meaning that the essence qualified by existence and all other attributes of perfection different from the Creator, exalted and sanctified, is impossible to exist in the external world. And know that even though they made this derivative issue agreed upon, it is only the opinion of those who say that the non-existent has an attribute.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is the fourth derivative issue (tafri') with its objection and answer, followed by a concluding note. The proponents assert that it is permissible to be certain that the world has a Creator qualified by attributes even while still doubting His existence, pending proof. This is because they allow the non-existent (the Creator's essence before creation) to be qualified by positive attributes. The objector argues that this leads to absurdity: one could then doubt the existence of bodies even after knowing they are in motion or at rest, since they might have those attributes in non-existence. The answer provides two responses: first, once we conceive the essence with such attributes, doubt is permissible until proof; second, the very concept of the Maker is such that if He exists He would have those attributes, so doubt is natural. This reasoning does not depend on the doctrine of the non-existent being a thing; even those who deny that doctrine accept that the existence of the Necessary Being requires proof. The concluding note clarifies that this tafri' is only valid for those who hold that the non-existent has an attribute, not universally.

الشك
القطع
الصانع
المعدوم
الصفات الثبوتية
الواجب
شريك الباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Certainty and doubt cannot coexist regarding the same subject.
Correction: They are sequential: doubt before proof, certainty after; here attributes are certain while existence is doubted until proof.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's main criticism of the fourth derivative issue?
A) It implies that bodies need no proof for existence.
B) It implies that one can doubt the existence of bodies even after knowing their attributes.
C) It denies the attributes of the Creator.
D) It requires a separate proof for every existent.

Key Takeaway
The fourth derivative issue shows that attributes can be known independently of existence, but this view is only consistent for those who accept that the non-existent can have attributes.

What would change in your life if you moved from abstract attributes to a living relationship?

If you hold that God's attributes are real even when you are not certain He exists, then your doubt is about existence, not about who He is. First Proof: Non-Existence and Power
Sanusi presents a proof and its refutation with an objection-answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that non-existence contradicts power and how the Ash'ari refutes it by distinguishing between eternal essence and temporal existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence's affirmation does not contradict power; existence can be caused temporally.

Sanusi opens with a proof claiming that if non-existent things were affirmed, they could not be brought into existence by power, since their essence is pre-eternal. He then provides the Ash'ari refutation, arguing that existence can be a temporal effect of divine power. The passage also addresses an objection about infinite regress and answers by denying its impossibility for affirmed entities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof
Read the first sentence: why does affirmation of the non-existent contradict power? Notice the key terms: essence (dhat) is pre-eternal, existence is a state not attached by power.
The proof assumes that if something is pre-eternal, it cannot receive existence from power.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation counters by allowing the essence to be attributed with existence temporally through power. This separates the eternal essence from the temporal existence.
This is the core Ash'ari position: possibility can have pre-eternal essence but temporal existence.
3
Follow the objection and answer
An objection claims this leads to regress and attributing existence to the non-existent. The answer denies the impossibility of regress in affirmed (thabit) things and their attribution with existence.
This shows that regress is not inherently impossible for the class of affirmed entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: أن ثبوت المعدوم ينافي المقدورية، لأن الذات أزلية (٧) والوجود حال لا يتعلق به قدرة (٨)
ورد الأول : بجواز أن يكون اتصاف الذات بالوجود حادثاً بالقدرة (١) فإن قيل : هو منفي وإلا لزم التسلسل واتصاف المعدوم بالوجود.
أجيب بمنع استحالة التسلسل في الثابت واتصاف الثابت بالوجود.
First: that the affirmation of the non-existent contradicts power, because the essence is pre-eternal and existence is a state that no power attaches to. The first was refuted: by the possibility that the attribution of the essence with existence occurs temporally by power. If it is said: it is negated, otherwise there would be regress and attribution of the non-existent with existence, the answer is by denying the impossibility of regress in the affirmed and the attribution of the affirmed with existence.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This lesson covers the first proof against the existence of the non-existent and its refutation. The proof states that if the non-existent had affirmation, then it would contradict the possibility of power (qudra) bringing it into existence, because the essence (dhat) of a possible thing is pre-eternal (azali) in its thubut, and existence is a state that cannot be attached by power. The refutation responds that it is possible for the essence to be attributed with existence temporally by divine power. If an opponent objects that the attribution would then be negated (since existence would be a mere accident) leading to infinite regress, the answer is to deny the impossibility of regress in the affirmed things (thabit) and the attribution of existence to them.

Key Terms
المقدورية
الذات
الأزلية
القدرة
التسلسل
الثابت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that pre-eternal essence implies that existence must also be pre-eternal.
Correction: The Ash'ari position distinguishes between the pre-eternal thubut of the essence and the temporal existence caused by power.

Checkpoint
What is the key point in the refutation of the first proof?
A) Regress is impossible.
B) Essences are not pre-eternal.
C) Existence can be temporally caused by power.
D) Power cannot attach to essences.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school holds that essences are pre-eternally affirmed, but existence is a temporal accident caused by divine power, and infinite regress of such accidents is not impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument permits infinite regress of temporal events for an affirmed substrate. Reflect: your every action is a temporal event caused by God's power in your affirmed self. Are you living with the constant awareness that your existence is sustained instant by instant? What would it take for you to feel that dependence in your next breath?
?
The misconception that pre-eternal essence forces pre-eternal existence is corrected. Do you sometimes act as if your current state—good or bad—is fixed and eternal, forgetting that God can bring new existence into your essence at any moment? How can you actively expect His perpetual power to transform your situation or character right now?

Third Proof and Its Refutation: thubut vs wujud
Sanusi presents a proof and its refutation regarding thubut and tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming thubut for non-existent essences does not entail multiple necessary beings, and the key distinction between thubut and wujud.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being is independent in existence, not in thubut.

Sanusi presents a third argument that could imply multiple necessary beings if thubut of essences is not from another. The refutation clarifies a fundamental distinction: necessity pertains to existence, not to the mere affirmation of essences. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage, which states the proof: if thubut of essences is not from another, then multiple necessary beings would follow.
Notice the term 'thubut' (affirmation) which is central.
2
Examine the refutation
Read the second sentence, which provides the refutation: the Necessary Being is defined by independence in existence (wujud), not in thubut.
This refutation hinges on the distinction between thubut and wujud.
3
Understand the thubut/wujud distinction
Reflect on why the refutation works: thubut of possible essences is not existence; only existence requires independence from another to be necessary.
This distinction is critical in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن ثبوت الذوات عندكم ليس من الغير فيلزم تعدد الواجب .
والثالث : بأن الواجب ما يستغني عن الغير في وجوده لا ثبوته (٣).
Third: that the affirmation of essences according to you is not from another, so it necessitates multiplicity of the Necessary [Being]. And the third [refutation] is that the Necessary is that which is independent of another in its existence, not in its affirmation.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This lesson covers the third proof and its refutation. The proof argues that if non-existent essences have an affirmation (thubut) that is not caused by another (i.e., self-subsistent), then there would be multiple necessary beings. The refutation clarifies that the Necessary Being (al-wajib) is defined as that which is independent of another in its existence (wujud), not in its thubut. The thubut of possible things is not existence, so they are not necessary.

Key Terms
الذوات
الواجب
الثبوت
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that thubut of possible essences is a kind of existence that would make them necessary.
Correction: Thubut is not existence; necessity pertains only to independence in existence, not in thubut.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the Necessary Being from possible essences according to the refutation?
A) The Necessary Being has thubut.
B) The Necessary Being is independent in existence, not in thubut.
C) Possible essences are independent in thubut.
D) Thubut is equivalent to existence.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being is defined by independence in existence, not in the affirmation of its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if your own existence is self-sufficient, forgetting that your actual being depends on God every single moment? What would change in your daily routine if you truly internalized that only God is independent in existence?
?
The lesson distinguishes between 'thubut' (affirmation of essence) and 'wujud' (actual existence). In what area of your life do you content yourself with mere theoretical belief (thubut) without letting it transform your actual existence (wujud)? Name one step you can take this week to move from affirmation to living reality.
?
The proof assumes that self-subsistence in thubut would lead to multiple necessary beings. How does this remind you that only God is truly independent? Identify one person, possession, or habit you treat as 'necessary' for your well-being. Commit to reorienting your reliance entirely toward Allah.

The Proof Against Thubut from Divine Power's Scope
Taftazani's conditional proof against thubut

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key premise that if non-existent things were 'established' (thabit), then divine power could not affect any possible thing, and why this conclusion is considered false.

Core Idea
If non-existents are real, even God's power cannot touch them.

In this lesson, Taftazani presents a key argument against the view that non-existent things have a kind of reality (thubut). The argument is conditional: if they were established, then divine power could not affect them—a result that is obviously false. We'll explore the reasoning behind this entailment.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic beginning with 'منا من جعل نفي ثبوت المعدوم غير ضروري' through 'على ما سيأتي'. This presents the conditional argument.
Notice the structure: conditional statement, then justification of the entailment.
2
Understand the two options for power's effect
The proof says power's effect must be either in the eternal essence (which cannot be subject to power) or in existence as a 'state' (hal). States are not under power by consensus.
The concept of 'hal' is introduced as a middle ground between existence and non-existence.
3
Grasp why the conclusion is false
The argument is a reductio: if the consequent (impossibility of power) is false, then the antecedent (thubut of non-existents) cannot hold. Reflect on why this is considered compelling.
This relies on the accepted premise that divine power can affect all possible things.
4
Consider the attempted analogy
The passage mentions an attempt to compare this with accidents but dismisses it as weak. Note why this analogy fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منا من جعل نفي ثبوت المعدوم غير ضروري فاستدل عليه بوجوده الأول: لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً لامتنع تأثير القدرة في شيء من الممكنات واللازم باطل ضرورة واتفاقاً. وجه اللزوم أن التأثير إما في نفس الذات وهي أزلية، والأزلية تنافي المقدورية. وإما في الوجود وهو حال إما على المثبتين ،فإلزاماً، وإما على النافين فإثباتاً بالحجة على ما سيأتي . والأحوال ليست بمقدورة باتفاق القائلين بها ولأن عدم توقف لونية السواد وعالمية من فام به العلم على تأثير القدرة ضروري، وأما التمسك بأنه لو كان مثل عالمية العالم، ومتحركية المتحرك بالفاعل، لكن بدون العلم والحركة ويؤدي إلى إبطال القول بالأعراض، فلا يخفى ضعفه، ثم نفي المقدورية لا يستلزم ثبوت الأزلية، ليلزم أزليه الوجود، بل أزلية اتصاف الذات بالوجود بناء على كونه نسبة بينهما، لا يتوقف على غيرهما لأنهم يجوزون الثبوت بلا علة أو بعلة غير قادرة . وأجيب بمنع الحصر لجواز أن يكون تأثير القدرة في اتصاف الذات بالوجود، لا يقال الاتصاف منتف . أما أولاً : فلأنه لو ثبت لكان له اتصاف بثبوت وتسلسل . وأما ثانياً : فلما سبق من أنه ليس بين الماهية والوجود اتصاف بحسب الخارج، كما بين البياض والجسم. وإنما ذلك بحسب الذهن فقط، وإلا لزم اتصاف المعدوم بالوجود، لأن الماهية بدون الوجود لا تكون بحسب الخارج إلا معدومة، إذ الماهية من حيث هي هي إنما هي في التصور فقط لأنا نجيب عن الأول : بأنه لو سلم زيادة اتصاف الاتصاف بالثبوت على نفس الاتصاف فلا نسلم نفي استحالة التسلسل في الثابتات وإنما قام الدليل عليه في الموجودات. وعن الثاني : بأنا لا نسلم استحالة اتصاف المعدوم الثابت بالوجود، وصيرورته عند الاتصاف موجوداً بذلك الوجود، كالجسم الغير الأسود يتصف بالسواد، ويصير أسود بذلك السواد، وإنما يستحيل ذلك فيما ليس بثابت في الخارج، وهذا ما ذكره الإمام : من أن القول بثبوت المعدوم متفرع على
Among us is one who made the negation of thubut of the non-existent not self-evident, and he adduced for it by its existence the first [proof]: If the non-existent were established, then the effect of power (al-qudra) would be prevented regarding any of the possible things. And the consequent is false necessarily and by consensus. The aspect of the entailment is that the effect is either in the essence (al-dhat) itself, and it is eternal (azali), and eternity precludes being under power (maqduriyya); or it is in existence (al-wujud), and it is a state (hal)—either according to the affirmers [of states] as an implication (ilzaman), or according to the deniers as an establishment (ithbatan) by demonstration as will come later. And states are not subject to power by consensus of those who affirm them, and because the non-dependence of the quality of blackness and the quality of knowledge in the knower on the effect of power is necessary. As for the attempt that it would be like the knowledge of the knowing and the motion of the moving by the agent, but without knowledge and motion, and it leads to invalidating the doctrine of accidents, its weakness is not hidden. Then the negation of being under power does not entail the thubut of eternity, so that eternity of existence would be entailed; rather, eternity of the attribute of the essence with existence based on it being a relation (nisba) between them, not dependent on other than them, because they permit thubut without a cause or with a cause that is not powerful. And it was answered by denying the exclusivity for the permissibility that the effect of power is in the attribute of the essence with existence. It is not said that the attribute (al-ittisaf) is negated. As for the first [objection], because if it were established, it would have an attribute by thubut and regression (tasalsul). And as for the second [objection], because of what preceded, that there is no attribute (ittisaf) between the quiddity (al-mahiyya) and existence according to external [reality], as between whiteness and body. Rather, that is only according to the mind; otherwise, the attribute of the non-existent with existence would be necessitated, because the quiddity without existence is only non-existent according to external [reality], since the quiddity in itself is only in conception. For we answer the first [objection]: That if the increase of the attribute of the attribute (ittisaf al-ittisaf) by thubut over the attribute itself is granted, we do not grant the denial of the impossibility of regression in the established things (thabitat); rather, the proof for it is established in existent things. And the second [objection]: That we do not grant the impossibility of the attribute of the established non-existent with existence, and its becoming existent by that existence at the time of being attributed, like a non-black body being attributed with blackness and becoming black by that blackness. That is only impossible for what is not established in external [reality]. And that which the Imam mentioned is that the doctrine of thubut of the non-existent branches upon
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the view of thubut (establishedness) of non-existent things is not self-evident, and must be argued. The proof rests on a conditional: if the non-existent (al-ma'dum) were established (thabit), then divine power (al-qudra) could not affect any possible thing—a conclusion that is false by necessity and consensus. The justification for the entailment is that the effect of power must be either in the essence itself (which is eternal, and eternity precludes being under power) or in existence (which is a 'state' or 'mode' (hal)—a controversial concept). States are not subject to power by agreement of those who affirm them. The text then addresses an attempted analogy with accidents, but dismisses it as weak. Importantly, the negation of being under power does not imply eternity of existence; rather, it implies the eternity of the attribute of the essence with existence, based on the idea that this attribute is a relation that does not depend on anything else. In response, an objection is raised: the effect of power might be in the attribute (ittisaf) of the essence with existence (not in the essence or existence directly). This leads to a discussion: first, if that attribute were established, it would itself require an attribute by thubut, leading to regression (tasalsul). Second, it was argued earlier that there is no real external relation between quiddity (mahiyya) and existence—it is only mental. Otherwise, non-existent would have to be attributed with existence. The text then provides answers to these two objections. For the first, even if we grant the increase of levels of attribution, we do not deny the possibility of regression in the realm of established things (thabitat), only in existent things. For the second, we do not grant the impossibility of an established non-existent being attributed with existence and becoming existent thereby, analogous to a non-black body becoming black. This is only impossible for things not established externally. The passage ends by noting that this is what the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) stated: that the doctrine of thubut of the non-existent branches upon... (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
al-qudra
al-ma'dum
al-thabit
al-dhat
al-wujud
al-azali
al-mahiyya
al-ittisaf
tasalsul
hal

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof alone is sufficient to disprove thubut.
Correction: The proof is conditional and presupposes consensus on divine power's scope; the debate continues with objections and replies.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise in the proof that if non-existents were established, power could not affect them?
A) Power can only affect what is non-existent
B) The effect of power is either in the eternal essence or in existence as a state
C) Non-existence is eternal
D) Power creates existence from nothing

Key Takeaway
The initial conditional proof argues that if non-existents were established, they would be outside divine power, which is false; thus, the thubut doctrine faces a serious challenge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that some aspects of your past are 'established' in such a way that God's power cannot reach them—like a sin too great to forgive or a failure too deep to transform? This lesson argues that non-existents (including forgiven sins) are not real as 'thabit,' so nothing is beyond God's power. Will you let this truth melt your despair and turn to Him in surrender right now?
?
The proof hinges on the idea that your very existence at this moment is a state (hal) directly caused by God's power, not a self-sustaining fact. If He withdrew His power for an instant, you would cease to be. Have you been living as if your existence is autonomous and automatic? How will you consciously depend on God for your next breath?
?
If the non-existent were real, God's power would be blocked—this is the core reductio. Are there areas of your life where you act as if something 'non-existent' (a future possibility, a changed heart) is unreachable by God? Write down one specific thing you have given up hope for, and this day declare that God's power is not restricted by what is or is not 'thabit.'

Reply to the Objection: The Effect on the Attribute of Existence
Taftazani's reply to the objection of attribution and the regression argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine power might act on the attribute (ittisaf) of the essence with existence, and the counter-argument that this leads to infinite regression in the realm of 'established' things.

Core Idea
Even an attribute of existence would bring infinite regression if non-existents are established.

After presenting the initial proof, Taftazani anticipates an objection: perhaps divine power affects the 'attribution' of existence to the essence, not the essence itself. He responds by arguing that this attribution would itself require further attribution, leading to an infinite chain. However, he notes that infinite regression is not necessarily impossible for 'established' things, only for existent ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic from 'وأجيب بمنع الحصر' through 'وإنما قام الدليل عليه في الموجودات'. This contains the objection that power acts on the attribute (ittisaf) of essence with existence, and the reply addressing the regression argument.
Note the careful distinction between essence, existence, and the attribute (ittisaf).
2
Understand the regression argument
The reply claims that if the attribute (ittisaf) were established, it would need its own attribute by thubut, leading to an infinite regression (tasalsul).
This is a classic argument against increasing levels of attribution.
3
See the distinction between thabitat and existents
The reply concedes that regression is impossible for existent things but not for 'established' things (thabitat). The proof against regression only applies to existents.
This nuance suggests that the realm of thubut has different logical rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

What does this passage teach you about the danger of confusing mental distinctions with external realities when thinking about the Divine?
?
This lesson reveals that even the most rigorous logical argument (like regression) has its limits. When you rely on your own reasoning to understand God, at what point must you humbly submit to the revealed truths and accept that some things are beyond the grasp of logic? How will you consciously guard against intellectual pride in your study of aqeedah?

The Second Objection: Mental vs External Attribution
Taftazani's reply to the second objection and the analogy with blackness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the relation between quiddity and existence is purely mental, and Taftazani's reply that an established non-existent can be attributed with existence, just as a non-black body becomes black.

Core Idea
Non-existents can acquire existence by attribution, like a body acquires blackness.

The second objection to the reply is that there is no real external relation between quiddity and existence—it's only mental. Taftazani responds by affirming that an established non-existent can indeed be attributed with existence and become existent, comparing it to a non-black body becoming black. The impossibility only applies to things not established in external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and reply
Read the Arabic from 'وأما ثانياً' through the end of the provided text. This contains the objection about mental vs external and the reply with the analogy.
Note how the reply uses the analogy of a body acquiring blackness.
2
Understand the mental vs external distinction
The objection claims that there is no real external relation between quiddity and existence; it's only in the mind. This would make the attribution (ittisaf) problematic.
This is a key issue in ontology: are relations between quiddity and existence real or mental?
3
Grasp the analogy and its force
Taftazani replies by saying that an established non-existent can be attributed with existence, just as a non-black body is attributed with blackness and becomes black. The only impossibility is for things not established in external reality.
The analogy shows that change is possible through attribution.
4
Note the incompleteness
The passage ends with a note that this is what the Imam (al-Razi) stated, and the text cuts off. Reflect on what this implies about the ongoing debate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منا من جعل نفي ثبوت المعدوم غير ضروري فاستدل عليه بوجوده الأول: لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً لامتنع تأثير القدرة في شيء من الممكنات واللازم باطل ضرورة واتفاقاً. وجه اللزوم أن التأثير إما في نفس الذات وهي أزلية، والأزلية تنافي المقدورية. وإما في الوجود وهو حال إما على المثبتين ،فإلزاماً، وإما على النافين فإثباتاً بالحجة على ما سيأتي . والأحوال ليست بمقدورة باتفاق القائلين بها ولأن عدم توقف لونية السواد وعالمية من فام به العلم على تأثير القدرة ضروري، وأما التمسك بأنه لو كان مثل عالمية العالم، ومتحركية المتحرك بالفاعل، لكن بدون العلم والحركة ويؤدي إلى إبطال القول بالأعراض، فلا يخفى ضعفه، ثم نفي المقدورية لا يستلزم ثبوت الأزلية، ليلزم أزليه الوجود، بل أزلية اتصاف الذات بالوجود بناء على كونه نسبة بينهما، لا يتوقف على غيرهما لأنهم يجوزون الثبوت بلا علة أو بعلة غير قادرة . وأجيب بمنع الحصر لجواز أن يكون تأثير القدرة في اتصاف الذات بالوجود، لا يقال الاتصاف منتف . أما أولاً : فلأنه لو ثبت لكان له اتصاف بثبوت وتسلسل . وأما ثانياً : فلما سبق من أنه ليس بين الماهية والوجود اتصاف بحسب الخارج، كما بين البياض والجسم. وإنما ذلك بحسب الذهن فقط، وإلا لزم اتصاف المعدوم بالوجود، لأن الماهية بدون الوجود لا تكون بحسب الخارج إلا معدومة، إذ الماهية من حيث هي هي إنما هي في التصور فقط لأنا نجيب عن الأول : بأنه لو سلم زيادة اتصاف الاتصاف بالثبوت على نفس الاتصاف فلا نسلم نفي استحالة التسلسل في الثابتات وإنما قام الدليل عليه في الموجودات. وعن الثاني : بأنا لا نسلم استحالة اتصاف المعدوم الثابت بالوجود، وصيرورته عند الاتصاف موجوداً بذلك الوجود، كالجسم الغير الأسود يتصف بالسواد، ويصير أسود بذلك السواد، وإنما يستحيل ذلك فيما ليس بثابت في الخارج، وهذا ما ذكره الإمام : من أن القول بثبوت المعدوم متفرع على
Among us is one who made the negation of thubut of the non-existent not self-evident, and he adduced for it by its existence the first [proof]: If the non-existent were established, then the effect of power (al-qudra) would be prevented regarding any of the possible things. And the consequent is false necessarily and by consensus. The aspect of the entailment is that the effect is either in the essence (al-dhat) itself, and it is eternal (azali), and eternity precludes being under power (maqduriyya); or it is in existence (al-wujud), and it is a state (hal)—either according to the affirmers [of states] as an implication (ilzaman), or according to the deniers as an establishment (ithbatan) by demonstration as will come later. And states are not subject to power by consensus of those who affirm them, and because the non-dependence of the quality of blackness and the quality of knowledge in the knower on the effect of power is necessary. As for the attempt that it would be like the knowledge of the knowing and the motion of the moving by the agent, but without knowledge and motion, and it leads to invalidating the doctrine of accidents, its weakness is not hidden. Then the negation of being under power does not entail the thubut of eternity, so that eternity of existence would be entailed; rather, eternity of the attribute of the essence with existence based on it being a relation (nisba) between them, not dependent on other than them, because they permit thubut without a cause or with a cause that is not powerful. And it was answered by denying the exclusivity for the permissibility that the effect of power is in the attribute of the essence with existence. It is not said that the attribute (al-ittisaf) is negated. As for the first [objection], because if it were established, it would have an attribute by thubut and regression (tasalsul). And as for the second [objection], because of what preceded, that there is no attribute (ittisaf) between the quiddity (al-mahiyya) and existence according to external [reality], as between whiteness and body. Rather, that is only according to the mind; otherwise, the attribute of the non-existent with existence would be necessitated, because the quiddity without existence is only non-existent according to external [reality], since the quiddity in itself is only in conception. For we answer the first [objection]: That if the increase of the attribute of the attribute (ittisaf al-ittisaf) by thubut over the attribute itself is granted, we do not grant the denial of the impossibility of regression in the established things (thabitat); rather, the proof for it is established in existent things. And the second [objection]: That we do not grant the impossibility of the attribute of the established non-existent with existence, and its becoming existent by that existence at the time of being attributed, like a non-black body being attributed with blackness and becoming black by that blackness. That is only impossible for what is not established in external [reality]. And that which the Imam mentioned is that the doctrine of thubut of the non-existent branches upon
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the view of thubut (establishedness) of non-existent things is not self-evident, and must be argued. The proof rests on a conditional: if the non-existent (al-ma'dum) were established (thabit), then divine power (al-qudra) could not affect any possible thing—a conclusion that is false by necessity and consensus. The justification for the entailment is that the effect of power must be either in the essence itself (which is eternal, and eternity precludes being under power) or in existence (which is a 'state' or 'mode' (hal)—a controversial concept). States are not subject to power by agreement of those who affirm them. The text then addresses an attempted analogy with accidents, but dismisses it as weak. Importantly, the negation of being under power does not imply eternity of existence; rather, it implies the eternity of the attribute of the essence with existence, based on the idea that this attribute is a relation that does not depend on anything else. In response, an objection is raised: the effect of power might be in the attribute (ittisaf) of the essence with existence (not in the essence or existence directly). This leads to a discussion: first, if that attribute were established, it would itself require an attribute by thubut, leading to regression (tasalsul). Second, it was argued earlier that there is no real external relation between quiddity (mahiyya) and existence—it is only mental. Otherwise, non-existent would have to be attributed with existence. The text then provides answers to these two objections. For the first, even if we grant the increase of levels of attribution, we do not deny the possibility of regression in the realm of established things (thabitat), only in existent things. For the second, we do not grant the impossibility of an established non-existent being attributed with existence and becoming existent thereby, analogous to a non-black body becoming black. This is only impossible for things not established externally. The passage ends by noting that this is what the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) stated: that the doctrine of thubut of the non-existent branches upon... (the text cuts off).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attribution of existence to a non-existent is always impossible.
Correction: According to Taftazani, it is possible for an established non-existent (thabit), just as a body can acquire blackness.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Taftazani use to support the possibility of an established non-existent being attributed with existence?
A) A body becoming white
B) A non-black body becoming black
C) Existence being added to quiddity
D) A state (hal) changing

Key Takeaway
An established non-existent can be attributed with existence and become existent, just as a non-black body can become black, contrary to the objection that such attribution is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is an acquired attribute, like blackness on a body, what does that mean for your self-worth? Are you more attached to the existence you've been given than to the Giver of that existence? How would your daily priorities change if you deeply felt that you could have not been?
?
The passage argues that a non-existent can become existent by attribution. Have you ever reflected on the fact that you were once a non-existent, and then God attributed existence to you? Does that thought fill you with a sense of utter dependence, or do you take it for granted? What would it take for you to live as if your existence is a gift every moment?
?
When you consider the analogy of blackness, think of a quality you dislike in yourself. If a non-black body can become black by receiving blackness, can you receive a quality of character by permission of the One who gives existence? What is stopping you from seeking the attribute of taqwa (God-consciousness) if the same God who gave you existence can also give you that quality?

The Fourth Argument: Finitude of Non-Existent Essences and the Response
Al-Razi presents a complete argument-and-response dialectic on the finitude of non-existent essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument for the finitude of non-existent essences, the objections raised against it, and the logical rebuttals that defend the possibility of infinite non-existent essences.

Core Idea
Infinities do not behave like finite quantities; adding a finite to an infinite does not yield finitude.

Al-Razi continues the series of proofs concerning the non-existent. The fourth argument attempts to show that postulating infinite non-existent essences leads to contradiction because a finite part has come into existence. However, the response uncovers a subtle misunderstanding about the nature of infinity. This lesson walks through the argument, the objection, and the final answer, revealing the sophistication of kalam logic when dealing with infinites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the entire dialectical exchange. Notice how the argument is presented, then answered, then an objection is raised, and finally a rebuttal is given.
This passage is a complete logical unit; try to follow the flow of reasoning.
2
Identify the argument and response
Identify the proponent's argument: non-existent essences cannot be infinite because a finite part has emerged into existence, making the whole finite. Then note the response: adding a finite to an infinite does not necessarily make it finite.
The response challenges the assumption that 'finite + infinite = finite'.
3
Analyze the objection and its rebuttal
The objection states that the remaining non-existent part is less than the whole, so it must be finite. The rebuttal uses the example of ranks of numbers (e.g., natural numbers and even numbers) to show that among infinites, concepts of increase and decrease do not apply.
This is a classic move: showing that infinite sets can be compared without implying finitude.
4
Consider the final point
Even if the argument were valid, it would not disprove the establishment (thubut) of the non-existent essences, which is the real issue. This shows that the argument is not decisive.
The lesson ends with a reminder of what is truly at stake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن الذوات الثابتة والعدم غير متناهية عندكم، هذا محال لأن القدر الذي خرج منها إلى الوجود متناه اتفاقاً، فيكون الكل أكثر من القدر الذي بقي على العدم بقدر متناه وهو القدر الذي دخل في الوجود، فيكون الكل متناهياً بكونه زائداً على الغير بقدر متناه . وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم أن الزائد على الغير بقدر متناه يكون متناهياً، وإنما يكون كذلك لو كان ذلك الغير متناهياً وليس كذلك، لأن الباقية على العدم أيضاً غير متناهية كالكل . فإن قيل : هي أقل من الكل قطعاً، فينقطع عند التطبيق فيتناهى ويلزم تناهي الكل. فالجواب : النقض بمراتب الأعداد ، ومنع الزيادة والنقصان فيما بين غير المتناهين. ولو سلم فلا يلزم من بطلان القول بعدم تناهيها بطلان القول بثبوتها .
The fourth: That the established essences and non-existence are according to you infinite. This is impossible because the amount that emerged from them into existence is finite by agreement. Thus the whole is greater than the amount that remained in non-existence by a finite amount, namely the amount that entered into existence. So the whole is finite by virtue of being in excess of the other by a finite amount. And it was answered: We do not accept that that which is in excess of another by a finite amount is finite. It would be so only if that other were finite, but it is not so, because that which remains in non-existence is also infinite like the whole. For it is said: It is certainly less than the whole, so it terminates upon application, thus it becomes finite, and the finitude of the whole follows. The answer: The rebuttal by the ranks of numbers, and the denial of increase and decrease among infinites. Even if it is conceded, the invalidity of the statement of their infinity does not entail the invalidity of the statement of their establishment.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage presents a complete argument-and-response unit within a broader discussion on the eternity of non-existent essences. The fourth argument attempts to prove that the non-existent cannot be infinite because the part that has come into existence is finite, and adding it to the infinite remainder would make the whole finite—a contradiction if the whole is supposed to be infinite. The response denies the underlying assumption that adding a finite amount to an infinite yields a finite, because the infinite part remains infinite regardless. An objection is then raised: if the remaining non-existent is less than the whole, then when paired it would become finite, forcing the whole to be finite. The answer counters by citing the example of numbers: among infinites, concepts of increase, decrease, and finitude do not apply. Even if the argument were valid, it would not disprove the establishment (thubut) of the essences, which is the real point at issue. This lesson demonstrates the kind of logical maneuvering typical in kalam concerning infinites and existence.

العدم
تناهي

غير المتناهي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If you add a finite amount to an infinite amount, the total becomes finite.
Correction: The passage explicitly denies this (لا نسلم أن الزائد على الغير بقدر متناه يكون متناهياً). Among infinites, the concept of addition does not necessarily yield finitude, as shown by the ranks of numbers.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fourth argument, why does adding the finite existent part to the infinite non-existent part not make the whole finite?
A) Because the infinite part is not actual.
B) Because the infinite part is also finite in another sense.
C) Because increase and decrease do not apply among infinites.
D) Because the finite part is not actually added.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument fails because infinites do not obey the same rules of increase and decrease as finite quantities, and even if it succeeded, it would not disprove the establishment of non-existent essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this argument, al-Razi shows that a seemingly decisive logical objection can be rebutted by examining the nature of infinities. Where in your own reasoning about God do you assume that your finite logic applies without correction? How might you guard against overconfident conclusions?
?
The passage concludes that even if the infinitude of non-existent essences were disproven, their establishment would remain. Are there truths about God that you have rejected simply because you found them difficult to prove? How does this lesson challenge you to separate the proof from the truth?
?
Consider the intellectual humility required to say 'we do not accept that' when faced with a seemingly strong argument. Is there an area of your Islamic belief where you have accepted a popular argument uncritically? What would it mean to examine it as carefully as al-Razi does here?

The Original Fifth Argument: Three Logical Cases
Al-Razi enumerates three logical cases and their consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of al-Razi's fifth argument, which examines three possible relations between the non-existent (al-ma'dum) and the negated (al-manfi) and shows each leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
The non-existent cannot be both more general than the negated and apply to it without contradiction.

Imam al-Razi opens this argument by considering the relationship between two key concepts: the non-existent (al-ma'dum) and the negated (al-manfi). He systematically tests three possibilities—equality, specificity, and generality—and for each, derives an absurdity from the premise that nothing negated is established (thabt). This argument is a classic example of the logical methods used in kalam to probe the nature of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the original argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'al-khamis' to 'wa-huwa muhal' once, then read the translation. Notice how al-Razi sets up a case analysis with three possibilities.
Pay attention to the logical connective 'in' (if) for each case.
2
Identify the three cases
Find where al-Razi says 'equal to it', 'more specific than it', and 'more general than it'. For each, follow the reasoning that leads to a conclusion about whether the non-existent is established.
The first two cases lead straight to the conclusion that the non-existent is not established, because the negated is not established.
3
Examine the third case
Notice that for the 'more general' case, al-Razi argues that if it were a pure negation, there would be no difference from the specific case. So it must be established, which then leads to a contradiction when applied to the negated.
This is the key step that generates the syllogism.
4
Review the syllogism
Al-Razi then summarizes the contradictory result: 'Every negation is a non-existent, every non-existent is established, so every negation is established.' Identify why this is impossible.
The impossibility arises from the self-evident truth that a thing and its negation cannot both apply to the same subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أن المعدوم إما مساو للمنفي أو أخص منه أو أعم، إذ لا تباين لظهور التصادق، فإن كان مساوياً له أو أخص صدق كل معدوم منفي، ولا شيء من المنفي بثابت، فلا شيء من المعدوم بثابت. وإن كان أعم لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، وإلا لما بقي فرق بين العام والخاص بل ثابتاً. وقد صدق على المنفي فيلزم كونه ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الأمر الثابت ثابت، وهو باطل ضرورة استحالة صدق أحد النقيضين على الآخر، هذا تقرير الإمام على اختلاف عباراته، وقد اعتبر في بعضها النسب بين العدم والنفي، ثم قال : وإذا لم يكن العدم نفياً صرفاً بل ثابتاً وهو صادق على النفي انتظم قياس :هكذا كل نفي عدم وكل عدم ثابت فكل نفي ثابت. وهو محال . وأجيب عنه : بعبارات محصلها أنا لا نسلم أنه إذا لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، كان ثبوتياً محضاً. لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً بكون بعض أفراده ثابتاً كالمعدومات الممكنة، وبعضها منفياً كالممتنعات، وهذا القدر كاف في الفرق، وحينئذ لا يصدق أن كل معدوم ثابت ليلزم كون المنفي ثابتاً وزعم صاحب المواقف أن الاستدلال إلزامي . تقريره : أنه لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً كان المعدوم أعم من المنفي، وكان متميزاً عنه، فكان ثابتاً، لأن كل متميز ثابت عندكم، وقد صدق المعدوم على المنفي فيكون ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الوصف الثبوتي فهو ثابت ولا خفاء في أن الجواب المذكور لا يتأتى على هذا التقدير، فمن أورده لم يتفطن بمراد المستدل، وكون كلامه إلزامياً . فنقول : الجواب المذكور إنما أورد على تقرير الإمام، ولا أثر فيه لحديث الإلزام، على أنه لو قصد ذلك لكانت أكثر المقدمات لغواً. إذ يكفي أن يقال : لو لم يكن المعدوم والمنفي واحداً لكان المنفي متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً، على أن الحق أنه لا تعلق لهذا الإلزام بكون المعدوم ثابتاً. إذ يقال: لو كان المنفي مبايناً للموجود كان متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً. وليت شعري كيف جعل خصوص المعدوم مستلزماً لكونه منفياً، وعمومه مستلزماً لكونه ثابتاً مع قيام التميز في الحالين فإن قيل على التقريرين : لما كان زعم الخصم ثبوت
The fifth: That the non-existent is either equal to the negated, or more specific than it, or more general than it, since there is no contrast due to the evident coextension. If it is equal to it or more specific, then every non-existent is negated, and nothing that is negated is established, so nothing that is non-existent is established. And if it is more general, it is not a pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between the general and the specific, but rather it is established. And it applies to the negated, so it follows that it is established by necessity, because that to which the established attribute applies is established. This is the elaboration of the Imam according to his various expressions, and he considered in some of them the relations between non-existence and negation. Then he said: If non-existence is not a pure negation but rather established, and it applies to negation, then a syllogism is formed as follows: Every negation is a non-existent, and every non-existent is established, so every negation is established. And that is impossible. And it was answered by expressions whose gist is that we do not accept that if it is not a pure negation, it is a pure positive. It is possible that it is a concept whose some instances are established, like possible non-existents, and some are negated, like impossible ones. This much is sufficient for the distinction. And then it is not true that every non-existent is established, so that the negated would be established. And the author of al-Mawaqif claimed that the inference is ilzami (compelling). Its elaboration: If the non-existent were established, then the non-existent would be more general than the negated, and it would be distinct from it, so it would be established, because every distinct thing is according to you established. And the non-existent applies to the negated, so it would be established by necessity, because that to which the positive description applies is established. And it is not hidden that the aforementioned answer does not apply on this assumption. So whoever presented it did not understand the intent of the inferrer, and that his statement is ilzami. So we say: The aforementioned answer was only presented on the elaboration of the Imam, and there is no effect in it of the talk of ilzam. On the contrary, if he intended that, most of the premises would be superfluous. For it suffices to say: If the non-existent and the negated were not one, then the negated would be distinct from it, and would be established. Moreover, the truth is that this ilzam has no relation to the non-existent being established. For it is said: If the negated were distinct from the existent, it would be distinct from it, and would be established. And I wonder how he made the particularity of the non-existent entail its being negated, and its generality entail its being established, while distinctness exists in both cases. For if it is said on the two elaborations: Since the opponent claims the establishment
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-khamis) concerning the ontological status of the non-existent (al-ma'dum) in relation to the negated (al-manfi). The argument, attributed to Imam al-Razi, proceeds by cases: if al-ma'dum is coextensive with or more specific than al-manfi, then since nothing negated is established, al-ma'dum cannot be established. If it is more general, then it cannot be a pure negation (otherwise no difference from the specific case) and must be something established; but since al-ma'dum applies to al-manfi, al-manfi would then be established—an absurdity. The passage then presents an answer: the dichotomy is false; al-ma'dum can be a concept with both established instances (possible non-existents) and negated instances (impossible ones). The author of al-Mawaqif (al-Iji) then interjects, claiming that the argument is ilzami—it forces the opponent's own premises to a contradiction. He rephrases it: if al-ma'dum were established, it would be more general than al-manfi and distinct; distinctness implies establishment, and since al-ma'dum applies to al-manfi, al-manfi must be established. The original answer fails on this reading. The text then defends the original answer, arguing that it was addressing al-Razi's formulation, not the ilzami one, and that even if ilzami were intended, a simpler objection suffices: if the two are not identical, the negated is distinct and therefore established, which is a more direct objection. The passage ends mid-argument, but it illustrates the layered nature of kalam dialectic, with multiple authors and levels of response.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the non-existent is always a pure negation, with no positive aspect.
Correction: The third case shows that if the non-existent is more general than the negated, it cannot be a pure negation; it must be an established attribute, implying that the non-existent has a positive conceptual content.

Checkpoint
According to the third case in al-Razi's argument, if the non-existent is more general than the negated, what must be true?
A) It is a pure negation.
B) It is an established (positive) attribute.
C) It is the same as the negated.
D) It has no instances.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's fifth argument shows that the relationship between the non-existent and the negated leads to a contradiction unless one denies that the non-existent is a single univocal concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that assuming 'non-existence' is a simple, single concept leads to absurdity. What areas of your life have you treated as simple and univocal (e.g., 'failure', 'impossibility', 'sin') without acknowledging their complexity? Will you commit to examining one such concept with logical rigor this week?
?
Al-Razi uses a logical case analysis to avoid contradiction. Consider a belief you hold that you have never tested with such careful reasoning. Are you willing to subject it to the same scrutiny? What is at stake if you do not?
?
The conclusion of this argument is that a seemingly innocent assumption about a concept leads to an impossible outcome. How might you be holding onto assumptions about God, yourself, or the world that, upon deeper analysis, lead to contradictions you've ignored? Will you confront one of those assumptions today?

The Answer and the Ilzami Reformulation
Answer to the argument, ilzami claim, and defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to al-Razi's fifth argument, al-Iji's claim that it is an ilzami (compelling) argument, and the defense of the original answer.

Core Idea
The non-existent is a concept with both established and negated instances; but this answer fails under the ilzami reading.

After presenting al-Razi's argument, the text provides a response that denies the dichotomy: the non-existent is not a pure negation or pure positive, but a concept that includes both established instances (possible non-existents) and negated instances (impossible ones). However, the author of al-Mawaqif (al-Iji) argues that the argument is actually an ilzami one—it forces the opponent's own premises to a contradiction. He rephrases the argument: if the non-existent were established, it would be more general and distinct from the negated; distinctness implies establishment, and since the non-existent applies to the negated, the negated must be established. The text then defends the original answer, noting that the ilzami reading was not intended and that a simpler objection suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Start with 'wa ujiba anhu' and read through the answer. Identify the key point: the non-existent is a concept with both established and negated instances.
The answer denies that if it is not a pure negation, it must be a pure positive.
2
Examine the ilzami claim
Read the section starting with 'wa za'ama sahib al-mawaqif' to understand al-Iji's claim. He argues that the argument is ilzami, meaning it uses the opponent's own premises to derive a contradiction.
The ilzami reformulation uses a different syllogism: if the non-existent is distinct from the negated, it is established (because distinctness implies establishment).
3
See the criticism of the original answer
Notice how ilzam points out that the original answer fails because it assumes the argument is not ilzami. The passage then defends the original answer.
The defense argues that the ilzami reading was not the one addressed, and that even if it were, a simpler objection exists.
4
Understand the defense's counter-critique
Read the final part where the text questions the validity of the ilzami linkage: 'how can the particularity entail negation and generality entail establishment?'
This shows that the ilzami itself may be problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أن المعدوم إما مساو للمنفي أو أخص منه أو أعم، إذ لا تباين لظهور التصادق، فإن كان مساوياً له أو أخص صدق كل معدوم منفي، ولا شيء من المنفي بثابت، فلا شيء من المعدوم بثابت. وإن كان أعم لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، وإلا لما بقي فرق بين العام والخاص بل ثابتاً. وقد صدق على المنفي فيلزم كونه ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الأمر الثابت ثابت، وهو باطل ضرورة استحالة صدق أحد النقيضين على الآخر، هذا تقرير الإمام على اختلاف عباراته، وقد اعتبر في بعضها النسب بين العدم والنفي، ثم قال : وإذا لم يكن العدم نفياً صرفاً بل ثابتاً وهو صادق على النفي انتظم قياس :هكذا كل نفي عدم وكل عدم ثابت فكل نفي ثابت. وهو محال . وأجيب عنه : بعبارات محصلها أنا لا نسلم أنه إذا لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، كان ثبوتياً محضاً. لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً بكون بعض أفراده ثابتاً كالمعدومات الممكنة، وبعضها منفياً كالممتنعات، وهذا القدر كاف في الفرق، وحينئذ لا يصدق أن كل معدوم ثابت ليلزم كون المنفي ثابتاً وزعم صاحب المواقف أن الاستدلال إلزامي . تقريره : أنه لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً كان المعدوم أعم من المنفي، وكان متميزاً عنه، فكان ثابتاً، لأن كل متميز ثابت عندكم، وقد صدق المعدوم على المنفي فيكون ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الوصف الثبوتي فهو ثابت ولا خفاء في أن الجواب المذكور لا يتأتى على هذا التقدير، فمن أورده لم يتفطن بمراد المستدل، وكون كلامه إلزامياً . فنقول : الجواب المذكور إنما أورد على تقرير الإمام، ولا أثر فيه لحديث الإلزام، على أنه لو قصد ذلك لكانت أكثر المقدمات لغواً. إذ يكفي أن يقال : لو لم يكن المعدوم والمنفي واحداً لكان المنفي متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً، على أن الحق أنه لا تعلق لهذا الإلزام بكون المعدوم ثابتاً. إذ يقال: لو كان المنفي مبايناً للموجود كان متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً. وليت شعري كيف جعل خصوص المعدوم مستلزماً لكونه منفياً، وعمومه مستلزماً لكونه ثابتاً مع قيام التميز في الحالين فإن قيل على التقريرين : لما كان زعم الخصم ثبوت
The fifth: That the non-existent is either equal to the negated, or more specific than it, or more general than it, since there is no contrast due to the evident coextension. If it is equal to it or more specific, then every non-existent is negated, and nothing that is negated is established, so nothing that is non-existent is established. And if it is more general, it is not a pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between the general and the specific, but rather it is established. And it applies to the negated, so it follows that it is established by necessity, because that to which the established attribute applies is established. This is the elaboration of the Imam according to his various expressions, and he considered in some of them the relations between non-existence and negation. Then he said: If non-existence is not a pure negation but rather established, and it applies to negation, then a syllogism is formed as follows: Every negation is a non-existent, and every non-existent is established, so every negation is established. And that is impossible. And it was answered by expressions whose gist is that we do not accept that if it is not a pure negation, it is a pure positive. It is possible that it is a concept whose some instances are established, like possible non-existents, and some are negated, like impossible ones. This much is sufficient for the distinction. And then it is not true that every non-existent is established, so that the negated would be established. And the author of al-Mawaqif claimed that the inference is ilzami (compelling). Its elaboration: If the non-existent were established, then the non-existent would be more general than the negated, and it would be distinct from it, so it would be established, because every distinct thing is according to you established. And the non-existent applies to the negated, so it would be established by necessity, because that to which the positive description applies is established. And it is not hidden that the aforementioned answer does not apply on this assumption. So whoever presented it did not understand the intent of the inferrer, and that his statement is ilzami. So we say: The aforementioned answer was only presented on the elaboration of the Imam, and there is no effect in it of the talk of ilzam. On the contrary, if he intended that, most of the premises would be superfluous. For it suffices to say: If the non-existent and the negated were not one, then the negated would be distinct from it, and would be established. Moreover, the truth is that this ilzam has no relation to the non-existent being established. For it is said: If the negated were distinct from the existent, it would be distinct from it, and would be established. And I wonder how he made the particularity of the non-existent entail its being negated, and its generality entail its being established, while distinctness exists in both cases. For if it is said on the two elaborations: Since the opponent claims the establishment
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-khamis) concerning the ontological status of the non-existent (al-ma'dum) in relation to the negated (al-manfi). The argument, attributed to Imam al-Razi, proceeds by cases: if al-ma'dum is coextensive with or more specific than al-manfi, then since nothing negated is established, al-ma'dum cannot be established. If it is more general, then it cannot be a pure negation (otherwise no difference from the specific case) and must be something established; but since al-ma'dum applies to al-manfi, al-manfi would then be established—an absurdity. The passage then presents an answer: the dichotomy is false; al-ma'dum can be a concept with both established instances (possible non-existents) and negated instances (impossible ones). The author of al-Mawaqif (al-Iji) then interjects, claiming that the argument is ilzami—it forces the opponent's own premises to a contradiction. He rephrases it: if al-ma'dum were established, it would be more general than al-manfi and distinct; distinctness implies establishment, and since al-ma'dum applies to al-manfi, al-manfi must be established. The original answer fails on this reading. The text then defends the original answer, arguing that it was addressing al-Razi's formulation, not the ilzami one, and that even if ilzami were intended, a simpler objection suffices: if the two are not identical, the negated is distinct and therefore established, which is a more direct objection. The passage ends mid-argument, but it illustrates the layered nature of kalam dialectic, with multiple authors and levels of response.

المنفي
الثبوت
النفي
الإلزام
الممكن
الممتنع
التميز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the answer to the fifth argument is final and unassailable.
Correction: Al-Iji's ilzami claim shows that the answer fails if the argument is recast in ilzami form, forcing a re-examination of the dialectic.

Checkpoint
What is the gist of the answer to the fifth argument according to the passage?
A) The non-existent is always a pure negation.
B) The non-existent is a concept that can have both established and negated instances.
C) The non-existent is always established.
D) The non-existent is identical to the negated.

Key Takeaway
The dialectic over the non-existent demonstrates how kalam arguments can be refined through ilzami reformulations, requiring careful attention to the intended logical structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you debate matters of faith, do you truly listen to your opponent's argument, or do you prepare answers before understanding their point? How might this lesson's emphasis on ilzami reasoning challenge you to be more precise in your own responses?
?
The ilzami method forces one to see contradictions in one's own premises. Are there any deeply held beliefs—religious or otherwise—that, if traced consistently, would lead to conclusions you reject? Are you willing to honestly examine them?
?
The author expresses wonder at someone's reasoning ('ليت شعري'). Do you aspire to that level of careful, critical thinking in your own understanding of Allah? What practical step can you take this week to sharpen your reasoning in one area of your faith?

First Argument from Possibility and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents the first argument from possibility and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalam argument for the necessity of a preponderant (murajjih) for contingent beings, and how Taftazani refutes it by denying the necessity and allowing for chance.

Core Idea
Possibility alone does not force a preponderant; chance is a logical alternative.

Taftazani opens with a classic argument: possibility (imkan) entails equality of existence and non-existence relative to the essence of the possible thing, so the occurrence of one without a preponderant would be contradictory. He then refutes this by arguing that equality relative to essence only precludes preponderance from essence, not from elsewhere, and that chance ('ittifaq) is a permissible explanation without a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once through to grasp the flow of argument and refutation.
Notice the compact style: the argument, answer, objection, and reply are all in a few lines.
2
Analyze the argument's structure
Identify the premise (possibility implies equality) and the conclusion (occurrence without preponderant is contradictory). Ask yourself: why would this be a problem for theologians?
3
Examine the answer
See how the answer distinguishes between equality with respect to essence and preponderance from essence. Understand that only preponderance from essence is impossible, not from an external source.
The answer does not deny the logical possibility of preponderance from outside.
4
Study the objection and reply
The objection claims that if preponderance is not from another, it must be from essence. The reply states that this is exactly the disputed point, and suggests that it could happen by chance without any cause.
The reply introduces 'ittifaq (agreement/chance) as a third possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: أن الإمكان يستلزم تساوي الوجود والعدم بالنسبة إلى ذات الممكن، وهذا معنى اقتضاء ماهية الممكن لتساوي الطرفين، ووقوع أحدهما بلا مرجح يستلزم رجحانه ، وهما متنافيان .
والجواب : أن التساوي بالنظر إلى الذات إنما ينافي الرجحان بحسب الذات وهو غير لازم .
فإن قيل : الترجح إذا لم يكن بالغير كان بالذات ضرورة أنه لا ثالث
قلنا : نفس المتنازع لجواز أن يقع بحسب الاتفاق من غير سبب .
The first: that possibility (al-imkan) entails equality of existence and non-existence with respect to the essence of the possible, and this is the meaning of the quiddity of the possible requiring equality of the two sides, and the occurrence of one of them without a preponderant (murajjih) entails its preponderance, and they are contradictory. The answer: that equality with respect to essence only contradicts preponderance with respect to essence, and that is not necessary. For if it is said: the preponderance (tarajjuh) if not by another, then it is by essence necessarily, for there is no third. We say: that is the very point in dispute, for it is permissible that it occur by agreement (ittifaq) without a cause.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the necessity of a preponderant for a contingent being and its refutation. The argument states: possibility implies that existence and non-existence are equal relative to the essence of the possible. Therefore, if one of them occurs without a preponderant, that constitutes a preponderance without a preponderant, which is contradictory. The answer: equality relative to essence only precludes preponderance relative to essence, but not preponderance from an external source. A further objection: if preponderance is not from another, it must be from essence. The reply: that is exactly what is disputed; it could occur by sheer chance without any cause.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that equality of possibility relative to essence necessitates that one side cannot occur without an external preponderant.
Correction: The passage shows that equality relative to essence only precludes preponderance from essence itself, not from outside, and chance is a logical possibility without any cause.

Checkpoint

A) Possibility implies equality, and occurrence without a preponderant is impossible.
B) Preponderance must be by essence.
C) The quiddity of the possible necessitates existence.
D) Chance is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first argument that possibility necessitates a preponderant is refuted by showing that equality relative to essence does not preclude chance or external causes.

How will you respond when others present such logical proofs?
?
Consider a time you demanded a reason for why something happened. This lesson suggests that occurrence can be without a cause. Second Argument from Preponderance and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents the second argument that preponderance is an event needing a cause and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that preponderance requires an agent and how it is refuted by showing preponderance is a rational necessity, not a real attribute.

Core Idea
Preponderance is not a real attribute but a rational necessity tied to the agent.

This passage presents the second argument for the necessity of a preponderant cause for the possible. It argues that preponderance is an event needing a substrate, and then answers by showing that preponderance is not a real attribute but the necessity (wujub) that the agent creates. Understanding this distinction is crucial for the kalam proof of God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: first the argument (from 'الثاني' to 'فلا بد منه'), then the answer (from 'والجواب' to the end).
The argument is short; the answer is longer and contains key distinctions.
2
Identify the core claim of the argument
The argument claims that preponderance is an event that comes into existence, so it must have a substrate. It cannot be the effect (since effect is later), so it must be the agent or something in the agent.
This leads to the conclusion that the preponderant (agent) is necessary.
3
Understand the refutation's key insight
The answer denies that preponderance is a real event prior to existence. Instead, it is simultaneous with existence. Even if granted, it would cause absurdities. The correct view is that preponderance is the necessity (wujub) that the agent creates—a rational relation, not a real attribute.
Imam al-Razi is cited to support that preponderance, if considered positive, is a rational concept denoting the agent's necessitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني؛ أن الممكن ما لم يترجح لم يوجد وترجحه أمر حدث بعد أن لم يكن فيكون وجودياً، ولا بد له من محل، وليس هو الأثر لتأخره عن الترجيح، فيكون هو المؤثر لعدم الثالث فلا بد منه
·
والجواب : أن الترجح مع الوجود لا قبله ، إذ لا يتصور رجحان الوجود : مع كون الواقع هو العدم ولو سلم فقيام ترجح وجود الممكن أو عدمه بالمؤثر، ضروري البطلان والمذكور في كلام الإمام فكان الترجح الوجوب، وهما متلازمان بناء على أن أحد الطرفين يمتنع وقوعه مع التساوي . فكيف مع المرجوحية. فالراجح لا يكون إلا واجباً، وهذا الوجوب متقدم على الوجود، أن وجود الممكن محفوف بوجوبين سابق ولاحق . وهو سيجيء من
على ما نسبة بين المؤثر والأثر، يسمى من حيث الإضافة إلى المؤثر إيجاباً، وإلى الأثر وجوباً، فمنع سبقه على الوجود وكونه وصفاً للمؤثر ليس بسديد، سيما وقد قال الإمام في المباحث المشرقية () : إنه على تقدير كونه ثبوتياً. فمعنى عروضه للمؤثر أنه يصير محكوماً عليه بوجوب يصير محكوماً عليه بوجوب أن يصدر عنه ذلك الأثر،
فالأولى منع كونه أمراً محققاً مفتقراً إلى ما يقوم به في الخارج، بل هو أمر عقلي قائم بالمتصور من الممكن عند الحكم بحدوثه .
The second: that the possible (al-mumkin) as long as it is not preponderated does not exist, and its preponderance (tarajjuh) is an event that occurs after it was not, so it is existential, and it must have a substrate, and it is not the effect because the effect is posterior to the preponderance, so it (the preponderance) would be the agent, since there is no third, so it is necessary. The answer: that the preponderance is with existence, not before it, for preponderance of existence cannot be conceived when what actually occurs is non-existence. And even if it is granted, then the subsistence of the preponderance of existence or non-existence of the possible in the agent is necessarily false. What is mentioned in the Imam's discourse is that preponderance is necessity (wujub), and they are concomitant, based on the fact that one of the two sides cannot occur with equality. How then with preponderance? So the preponderant cannot be but necessary, and this necessity precedes existence; indeed the existence of the possible is surrounded by two necessities, anterior and posterior. This will come later, according to what relation between agent and effect is called, from the perspective of relation to the agent, 'ijaab', and to the effect, 'wujub'. So preventing its precedence over existence and its being an attribute of the agent is not sound, especially since the Imam said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: 'If it is considered positive, then the meaning of its occurrence in the agent is that it becomes subject to a necessity that the effect should issue from it.' So the better view is to deny that it is a real thing needing a substrate in the external world; rather it is a rational matter subsisting in the conception of the possible when its occurrence is judged.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage contains the second argument for the necessity of a preponderant and its refutation. The argument runs: the possible, without preponderance, does not exist; preponderance is a temporal event, thus it is an existent attribute that needs a substrate. It cannot be the effect itself, as the effect comes after preponderance. Therefore, preponderance must be the agent (or inhere in the agent), otherwise an infinite regress results. The answer: preponderance is simultaneous with existence, not prior to it. Moreover, the idea of preponderance in the agent as a real attribute is false; rather, preponderance is the necessity (wujub) that the agent creates. This necessity is not a real attribute but a rational relation. The author cites Imam al-Razi's view that preponderance, if considered positive, is a rational concept denoting the agent's being necessitated to produce the effect.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Preponderance is a rational relation (wujub) that denotes the agent's necessitation to produce the effect; it is not a real attribute needing a substrate.

Key Takeaway
