Refuting the Deniers' Three Objections
Taftazani refutes three objections from those denying the need for a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three strongest objections raised against the need for a creator and how Taftazani refutes each one using key concepts of essential posteriority, will, and relative non-existence.

Core Idea
Deniers' strongest objections fail once we recognize that will is a genuine cause.

Taftazani now confronts the most powerful arguments of those who deny that the world needs a creator. These objections—concerning the timing of creation, apparent random choices, and the nature of non-existence—are not merely academic; they cut to the heart of whether we can rationally argue for God. By working through each objection and its refutation, we see how the theologians defend the coherence of creation and the role of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Let the structure of objections and answers sink in before analyzing.
Note that the passage begins by stating these are the 'strongest doubts' of the deniers.
2
Identify each objection
Find the three objections. The first involves timing; the second uses the example of a fleeing man choosing a path; the third concerns non-existence as an effect.
These objections are interconnected but address different logical angles.
3
Study each answer carefully
For each objection, see how Taftazani responds. Notice the key terms: 'essential posteriority', 'will', 'relative non-existence'.
Pay special attention to how the second answer distinguishes between 'preponderance without a specific cause' and 'preponderance without any cause'.
4
Reflect on the role of will
Consider how the concept of will (irada) serves as the cause in cases where no external specific cause is apparent. This is a central theological principle.
This insight links to the broader argument for a free, willful creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومن أقوى شبه المنكرين . أن التأخير حال الوجود، إيجاد للموجود، وحال العدم جمع بين النقيضين وأن الضرورة قاضية بوقع الترجيح بلا مرجح في مثل الهارب من السبع يسلك أحد الطريقين، والعطشان يشرب أحد الماءين مع التساوي، وأن العدم نفي محض لا يصلح أثراً .
والجواب عن الأول : أن المحال إيجاد الموجود بوجود حاصل، بغیر هذا الإيجاد، وهو غير لازم غايته أن الوجود يقارن الإيجاد بالزمان، وهو لا ينافي التأخر بالذات .
وعن الثاني: أن اللازم على تقدير التسليم ترجيح المختار أحد المتساويين، بلا مخصص لا الترجح بلا سبب .
فإن قيل : هذا الاختيار والترجيح وقع بلا سبب.
قلنا : ممنوع بل الإرادة التي من شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص.
وعن الثالث: أنه عدم مضاف مستند إلى عدم العلة . بمعنى أن العقل يحكم بأنه عدم لعدم علته، وأما التمسك بأن العلية لكونها نقيض اللاعلية ثبوتية. وكذا موصوفها، وبأن التأثير إمـا في الماهية، أو الوجود، أو الموصوفية ، والكل باطل ) لما سبق ، وبأنه لو وجدت المؤثر به أو الحاجة تسلسلت فضعفه ظاهر .
He said: Among the strongest of the doubts of the deniers: that delaying at the time of existence is bringing the existent into existence, and at the time of non-existence is combining two contradictories; and that necessity judges the occurrence of preponderance without a preponderant in the case of the one fleeing from a lion taking one of two paths, and the thirsty person drinking one of two waters despite equality; and that non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect. The answer to the first: that the impossible is bringing the existent into existence with an already existent existence, not this act of bringing into existence, and that is not necessary; at most, existence accompanies the act of bringing into existence in time, and that does not contradict essential posteriority. The answer to the second: that what is necessary, if it is granted, is the preponderance of the chosen among two equals without a specific cause (mukhasis), not preponderance without a cause. For if it is said: this choice and preponderance occurred without a cause. We say: that is denied; rather it is the will (iraada) whose nature is to give preponderance and specification (takhsis). The answer to the third: that it is a relative non-existence attributed to the absence of a cause, meaning that reason judges it to be non-existence because of the non-existence of its cause. As for the insistence that causality, being the contradictory of non-causality, is positive, and likewise its subject, and that effect is either in the quiddity, or existence, or state of qualification—all are false based on what preceded; and that if there existed an agent for it or a need, an infinite regress would result, and its weakness is apparent.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage refutes three objections of those who deny the need for a creator. The first objection: if the world is created in time, its existence either occurs at a time when it already exists (impossible) or at a time when it does not exist (contradiction). The answer: the impossibility is only of bringing into existence something that already exists with the same existence; but the act of creation can be simultaneous with existence in time, which does not entail essential priority. The second objection: empirical cases like a fleeing man choosing one of two equal paths show preponderance without a preponderant. The answer: such cases exhibit preponderance without a specific cause, not without any cause; the cause is the will, which inherently specifies. The third objection: non-existence is pure negation and cannot receive causality. The answer: non-existence is relative, depending on the absence of a cause; it is not a positive effect. Further arguments about causality being positive and effect categories are dismissed as already refuted.

إرادة
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Empirical cases of choice without an apparent external cause (e.g., choosing one of two equal paths) prove that preponderance can occur without any cause, disproving the need for a creator.
Correction: Taftazani shows that in such cases, the cause is the will, which naturally specifies one alternative. Preponderance does not occur without a cause; it occurs without a specific external cause, but internal will serves as the cause.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's answer to the second objection, what is the cause of preponderance in cases like the fleeing man choosing a path?
A) The absence of any cause
B) The man's rational deliberation
C) The will (irada)
D) The equality of the paths

Key Takeaway
The three objections of the deniers are refuted by clarifying that creation involves essential posteriority not temporal simultaneity, that will is the cause in seemingly random choices, and that relative non-existence is explained by absence of cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice where both options seem equally good – like which job offer to take or whom to marry – do you truly acknowledge that your will is a real cause accountable to God, or do you dismiss it as mere randomness? How does Taftazani's answer force you to own that choice as an act of will for which you will be questioned?
?
The deniers thought that without a visible external cause, there is no cause at all. When something good happens in your life and you cannot trace it to your own effort, do you instinctively credit 'luck' or 'chance' instead of recognizing it as the direct act of God's will? What would change in your gratitude if you consistently saw His will as the cause?
?
The third objection treats non-existence as nothing, but Taftazani says it is relative – the absence of a cause. When you experience a lack in your life – poverty, loneliness, failure – do you feel it as a neutral void, or do you realize it is because God did not will otherwise? How does that realization invite you to turn to Him as the only source of existence and goodness?

The Two Views on the Cause of Contingent's Need
Taftazani presents the two competing views on the cause of the contingent's need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views on what necessitates a contingent being's need for an effect: possibility (imkan) vs. origination (huduth), and the initial arguments and counterarguments.

Core Idea
The contingent's need is either due to its possibility or its origination — a fundamental divide in kalam.

Taftazani introduces the sixth topic by presenting the core debate: does the intellect judge need from the mere possibility of the thing, or from its temporal origination? He then sketches the positions of the philosophers and the mutakallimun, along with an initial exchange. This lesson sets the stage for the detailed arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the footnote markers interspersed in the Arabic; these are editorial glosses.
2
Identify the two main claims
Find where Taftazani states the philosophers' view (imkan) and the mutakallimun's view (huduth). Understand how each group defines 'need' and its basis.
3
Analyze the initial objection and answer
The mutakallimun object that imkan cannot be the cause because it is a modality posterior to need. The philosophers answer by clarifying that they speak of the intellect's observation, not an actual property. Reflect on this distinction.
This answer is crucial for understanding the later arguments.

المبحث السادس في موجب احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر (قال : المبحث السادس : العقل بحكم الاحتياج (۱) بمجرد ملاحظة كون الذات (۲) غير مقتضية للوجود والعدم (۳)، فيكون المحوج هو الإمكان . الحدوث مستقلاً (٤) أو شرطاً أو شطراً (*) كيف . والحدوث صفة للوجود المتأخر عن التأثر المتأخر عن الاحتياج؟ وكثير من المتكلمين عكسوا الدعوى، والدليل (٦) والابطال. فقالوا : العقل يحكم بالاحتياج بمجرد ملاحظة أن الشيء لم يكن فكان، فيكون المحوج هو الحدوث لا الإمكان (۷) ، كيف (۸) وهو كيفية نسبة الماهية إلى الوجود المتأخر عن الاحتياج. الجواب : بأنا لا نعني أن الإمكان يتحقق فيوجب احتياجاً، بل إن العقل يلاحظ الإمكان فيحكم بالاحتياج (۹) ، كما يقال علة الاحتياج إلى الخير هو التحيز (1) ، جوابهم بعينه، والاعتراض (۲) بأنه لا احتياج حال البقاء، لأن التأثير حال البقاء في الوجود، تحصيل الحاصل . قيل : وفي البقاء وفي أمر متجدد تأثير في غير الباقي جاز في الإمكان مع زيادة حال ما قبل الوجود، فإنه نفي محض. الجواب : أن معنى الاحتياج إلى المؤثر، توقف الوجود أو العدم أو استمراره على أمر ما. قد سبق أن الممكن محتاج إلى السبب . إلا أن ذلك عند الفلاسفة وبعض المتكلمين لإمكانه ، وعند قدماء المتكلمين لحدوثه، وقيل لإمكانه مع الحدوث، وقيل بشرط الحدوث. احتجت الفلاسفة على دعواهم بأن العقل إذا لاحظ كون الشيء غير مقتضي الوجود أو العدم بالنظر إلى ذاته. حكم بأن وجوده أو عدمه لا يكون إلا بسبب خارج وهو معنى الاحتياج، سواء لاحظ كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم أو لم يلاحظ واحتجوا على إبطال مذهب المخالف، بأن الحدوث وصف للوجود ومتأخر عنه لكونه عبارة عن مسبوقية الوجود بالعدم، والوجود متأخر عن تأثير المؤثر، وهو عن الاحتياج إليه، وهو عن علة الاحتياج وجزئها وشرطها، فلو كان الحدوث علة للاحتياج أو جزأها أو شرطها، لزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه بمراتب وعارضهم بعض المتكلمين، فقالوا : سبب الاحتياج هو الحدوث، لأن العقل إذا لاحظ كون الشيء مما (۳) يوجد بعد العدم، حكم باحتياجه إلى علة تخرجه من العدم إلى الوجود، وإن لم يلاحظ كونه غير ضروري الوجود والعدم، ولا يجوز أن يكون هو الإمكان، لأنه كيفية لنسبة(٤) الوجود إلى الماهية فيتأخر عن الوجود المتأخر عن التأثير المتأخر عن الاحتياج إلى المؤثر. والحق أن هذه العلة إنما هي بحسب العقل بمعنى أنه يلاحظ الإمكان (۱) أي باحتياج وجوده إلى المؤثر. (٢) أي ذات الشيء المحكوم عليها بالاحتياج غير مقتضيه للوجود . (۳) وذلك بأن يدرك بأن تلك الذات الملحوظة تقبل كلا منهما بدلاً عن الآخر كون الشيء لا يقتضي الوجود لذاته ولا العدم لذاته بل يقبل كلا منهما معنى الامكان . أن موجب الحاجة ليس هو الحدوث مستقلاً كما قيل إن مجرد إدراك أن الشيء ربما حدث (٤) بمعنى يوجب إدراك أنه لا بد من محدث . (۹) إلى المؤثر وملاحظة المتأخر ليستدل به على المتقدم صحيحة فيعود المتقدم متأخراً.
The sixth topic: on the cause of the contingent's need for the effect. (He said: The sixth topic: the intellect judges the need (1) merely by observing that the essence (2) does not necessitate existence or non-existence (3), so the necessitator is possibility (al-imkan). Origination (al-huduth) as independent (4) or as condition or as part (*) how? And origination is an attribute of existence posterior to the impression posterior to the need? And many of the mutakallimun reversed the claim, the proof (6) and the refutation. They said: the intellect judges the need merely by observing that the thing was not and then was, so the necessitator is origination, not possibility (7), how (8) while it is a modality of the relation of the essence to existence posterior to the need. The answer: we do not mean that possibility becomes actual and necessitates a need, but rather the intellect observes possibility and judges the need (9), as it is said the cause of need for good is accidentalness (al-tahayyuz) (1), their very answer. And the objection (2) that there is no need in the state of persistence, because the effect in the state of persistence in existence is a realization of the already realized. It was said: and in persistence and in a renewed matter there is an effect on what is not permanent; it is permissible in possibility with the addition of the state before existence, for it is pure negation. The answer: the meaning of need for the effect is the dependence of existence or non-existence or its continuation on something. It has preceded that the possible is in need of the cause. Except that that is with the philosophers and some of the mutakallimun due to its possibility, and with the early mutakallimun due to its origination, and it was said due to its possibility along with origination, and it was said with the condition of origination. The philosophers argued for their claim that when the intellect observes that a thing does not necessitate existence or non-existence by considering its essence, it judges that its existence or non-existence is only through an external cause, which is the meaning of need, whether it observes it being preceded by non-existence or not. And they argued for refuting the opponent's view, that origination is an attribute of existence and posterior to it because it is an expression of existence being preceded by non-existence, and existence is posterior to the effect of the effect (al-mu'aththir), and that is posterior to the need for it, and that is posterior to the cause of need and its part and its condition. So if origination were the cause of need or its part or its condition, then the thing would be posterior to itself by degrees. And some of the mutakallimun opposed them, saying: the cause of need is origination, because when the intellect observes that a thing comes to exist after non-existence, it judges its need for a cause that brings it from non-existence to existence, even if it does not observe it being non-necessary in existence and non-existence. And it is not permissible that it be possibility, because it is a modality of the relation of existence to the essence, so it is posterior to existence which is posterior to the effect which is posterior to the need for the effect. And the truth is that this cause is only according to the intellect, in the sense that it observes possibility.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is a detailed theological and philosophical discussion about what necessitates a contingent being's need for an efficient cause (mu'aththir). The author, Taftazani, begins by stating the sixth topic: the cause of the contingent's need for an effect. He presents the view of the philosophers and some mutakallimun that mere possibility (imkan) — i.e., the essence being equally receptive to existence and nonexistence — is sufficient for the intellect to judge that it needs an external cause. He then contrasts this with the view of many mutakallimun, who invert the claim: they hold that the intellect judges need from observing that the thing came into existence after nonexistence, i.e., temporal origination (huduth), not from possibility. The philosophers respond by clarifying that they do not mean that the realization of possibility itself creates a need; rather, the intellect, upon observing possibility, judges the need. They liken this to the way accidentalness (tahayyuz) is considered the cause of need for good. The discussion then addresses an objection: if need is due to origination, there would be no need during the state of persistence (baqa'), but the philosophers argue that the effect during persistence is merely a continuation, which would be a realization of the already realized. A counterobjection is raised that persistence could involve a renewed effect, but this is dismissed as pure negation. The correct meaning of need is defined as the dependence of existence or nonexistence or its continuation on something. The author then summarizes the positions: philosophers and some mutakallimun attribute the need to possibility, early mutakallimun to origination, and there are also intermediate views. Next, the philosophers present a formal proof: when the intellect considers a thing that in itself does not necessitate existence or nonexistence, it judges that an external cause is necessary, regardless of whether it observes the thing preceded by nonexistence. They also argue against the origination view by pointing out that origination is an attribute of existence and thus posterior to it; if it were the cause of need, there would be an infinite regress. Some mutakallimun counter that origination is the cause because the intellect, upon observing existence after nonexistence, judges the need for a cause, even without considering possibility. They also argue that possibility cannot be the cause because it is a modality of the relation of existence to essence, hence posterior to existence and thus cannot be the cause of need. Finally, Taftazani states that the truth is that the cause of need is only relative to the intellect: the intellect observes possibility and judges the need, meaning that the judgment is based on the intellect's consideration, not on an actual property of the thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility (imkan) itself necessitates need as an actual property of the thing.
Correction: The philosophers clarify that it is the intellect's observation of possibility, not the property itself, that leads to the judgment of need.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' answer, what does the intellect observe to judge that a contingent being needs an effect?
A) The thing's temporal origination (huduth)
B) The thing's essence being equally receptive to existence and non-existence (imkan)
C) The thing's accidentalness (tahayyuz)
D) The thing's need for a specific form

Key Takeaway
The philosophers hold that the intellect's observation of possibility triggers the judgment of need, while the mutakallimun hold that origination is the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at your own life: do you truly feel your absolute need for Allah every moment, or do you act as if you are independent? What would change if you internalized the fact that your very existence is merely possible and sustained by the Necessary Being?
?
The mutakallimun focus on origination—that everything that begins exists due to a cause. When you consider your own coming into existence and your spiritual rebirth, do you acknowledge that you are entirely dependent on Allah? How does that recognition impact your daily choices and your relationship with Him?

Detailed Arguments and the Truth
Taftazani presents the detailed arguments and the final truth on the cause of need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed arguments from both philosophers and mutakallimun on the cause of need, including the persistence objection, the infinite regress argument, and the final resolution.

Core Idea
The cause of need is a matter of what the intellect observes, not an actual attribute of the contingent.

Taftazani now presents deeper arguments: an objection about the state of persistence, the philosophers' formal proof and refutation of the huduth view via infinite regress, the mutakallimun's counterargument, and finally the truth that the cause is only according to the intellect's observation of possibility. This lesson completes the discussion of the sixth topic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the objections
Read the passage and identify the objection about persistence (baqa') and the response that defines need as dependence. Then trace the philosophers' proof and their argument against huduth.
Pay attention to the logical structure of the regress argument.
2
Understand the mutakallimun's counter
The mutakallimun argue that huduth is the cause because the intellect judges need from observing existence after non-existence, and they argue that imkan cannot be the cause due to posteriority.
3
Grasp the final resolution
Taftazani states that the truth is that the cause is only 'according to the intellect' – the intellect observes possibility and judges need. Reflect on how this reconciles the debate.

(٢) أي ذات الشيء المحكوم عليها بالاحتياج غير مقتضيه للوجود . (۳) وذلك بأن يدرك بأن تلك الذات الملحوظة تقبل كلا منهما بدلاً عن الآخر كون الشيء لا يقتضي الوجود لذاته ولا العدم لذاته بل يقبل كلا منهما معنى الامكان . أن موجب الحاجة ليس هو الحدوث مستقلاً كما قيل إن مجرد إدراك أن الشيء ربما حدث (٤) بمعنى يوجب إدراك أنه لا بد من محدث . (٦) سقط من (أ) و (ب) (الدليل). (٥) أي جزء من الموجب. (۷) ولا يصح أن يكون الإمكان هو المحوج وحده ولا معنياً في المحوج. (۸) وكيف يصح أن يكون الإمكان هو المحوج. (۹) إلى المؤثر وملاحظة المتأخر ليستدل به على المتقدم صحيحة فيعود المتقدم متأخراً.
The sixth topic: on the cause of the contingent's need for the effect. (He said: The sixth topic: the intellect judges the need (1) merely by observing that the essence (2) does not necessitate existence or non-existence (3), so the necessitator is possibility (al-imkan). Origination (al-huduth) as independent (4) or as condition or as part (*) how? And origination is an attribute of existence posterior to the impression posterior to the need? And many of the mutakallimun reversed the claim, the proof (6) and the refutation. They said: the intellect judges the need merely by observing that the thing was not and then was, so the necessitator is origination, not possibility (7), how (8) while it is a modality of the relation of the essence to existence posterior to the need. The answer: we do not mean that possibility becomes actual and necessitates a need, but rather the intellect observes possibility and judges the need (9), as it is said the cause of need for good is accidentalness (al-tahayyuz) (1), their very answer. And the objection (2) that there is no need in the state of persistence, because the effect in the state of persistence in existence is a realization of the already realized. It was said: and in persistence and in a renewed matter there is an effect on what is not permanent; it is permissible in possibility with the addition of the state before existence, for it is pure negation. The answer: the meaning of need for the effect is the dependence of existence or non-existence or its continuation on something. It has preceded that the possible is in need of the cause. Except that that is with the philosophers and some of the mutakallimun due to its possibility, and with the early mutakallimun due to its origination, and it was said due to its possibility along with origination, and it was said with the condition of origination. The philosophers argued for their claim that when the intellect observes that a thing does not necessitate existence or non-existence by considering its essence, it judges that its existence or non-existence is only through an external cause, which is the meaning of need, whether it observes it being preceded by non-existence or not. And they argued for refuting the opponent's view, that origination is an attribute of existence and posterior to it because it is an expression of existence being preceded by non-existence, and existence is posterior to the effect of the effect (al-mu'aththir), and that is posterior to the need for it, and that is posterior to the cause of need and its part and its condition. So if origination were the cause of need or its part or its condition, then the thing would be posterior to itself by degrees. And some of the mutakallimun opposed them, saying: the cause of need is origination, because when the intellect observes that a thing comes to exist after non-existence, it judges its need for a cause that brings it from non-existence to existence, even if it does not observe it being non-necessary in existence and non-existence. And it is not permissible that it be possibility, because it is a modality of the relation of existence to the essence, so it is posterior to existence which is posterior to the effect which is posterior to the need for the effect. And the truth is that this cause is only according to the intellect, in the sense that it observes possibility.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is a detailed theological and philosophical discussion about what necessitates a contingent being's need for an efficient cause (mu'aththir). The author, Taftazani, begins by stating the sixth topic: the cause of the contingent's need for an effect. He presents the view of the philosophers and some mutakallimun that mere possibility (imkan) — i.e., the essence being equally receptive to existence and nonexistence — is sufficient for the intellect to judge that it needs an external cause. He then contrasts this with the view of many mutakallimun, who invert the claim: they hold that the intellect judges need from observing that the thing came into existence after nonexistence, i.e., temporal origination (huduth), not from possibility. The philosophers respond by clarifying that they do not mean that the realization of possibility itself creates a need; rather, the intellect, upon observing possibility, judges the need. They liken this to the way accidentalness (tahayyuz) is considered the cause of need for good. The discussion then addresses an objection: if need is due to origination, there would be no need during the state of persistence (baqa'), but the philosophers argue that the effect during persistence is merely a continuation, which would be a realization of the already realized. A counterobjection is raised that persistence could involve a renewed effect, but this is dismissed as pure negation. The correct meaning of need is defined as the dependence of existence or nonexistence or its continuation on something. The author then summarizes the positions: philosophers and some mutakallimun attribute the need to possibility, early mutakallimun to origination, and there are also intermediate views. Next, the philosophers present a formal proof: when the intellect considers a thing that in itself does not necessitate existence or nonexistence, it judges that an external cause is necessary, regardless of whether it observes the thing preceded by nonexistence. They also argue against the origination view by pointing out that origination is an attribute of existence and thus posterior to it; if it were the cause of need, there would be an infinite regress. Some mutakallimun counter that origination is the cause because the intellect, upon observing existence after nonexistence, judges the need for a cause, even without considering possibility. They also argue that possibility cannot be the cause because it is a modality of the relation of existence to essence, hence posterior to existence and thus cannot be the cause of need. Finally, Taftazani states that the truth is that the cause of need is only relative to the intellect: the intellect observes possibility and judges the need, meaning that the judgment is based on the intellect's consideration, not on an actual property of the thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' argument, why can origination (huduth) not be the cause of need?
A) Because origination is a modality of the relation of essence to existence
B) Because origination is posterior to existence and would lead to an infinite regress
C) Because origination is not observed by the intellect
D) Because origination is only an accidental attribute

Key Takeaway
The truth is that the cause of need is 'according to the intellect' – the intellect observes possibility and judges need, reconciling the debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate your own existence, do you genuinely feel that you could just as easily not exist? If that feeling is absent, ask yourself: is your belief in Allah merely a conclusion of an argument, or is it born from a real, personal sense of contingency and need?
?
The philosophers argued that need is due to possibility regardless of whether you consider non-existence. Do you ever live as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that at every moment you depend on the Creator? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that your existence is not necessary in itself?
?
Taftazani concludes that the cause of need is 'according to the intellect'. Does this intellectual judgment translate into a heartfelt dependency on Allah in your prayers and struggles? If not, why is there a gap, and what step can you take today to bridge it?
